</div></td></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1066479718" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="infobox" style="border-collapse:collapse; border-spacing:0px; border:none; width:100%; margin:0px; font-size:100%; clear:none; float:none"><tbody><tr><th class="infobox-header" colspan="2" style="text-align:left">Names</th></tr><tr><td class="infobox-full-data nickname" colspan="2" style="text-align:left; padding-left:0.7em;">Alexander III of Macedon</td></tr></tbody></table></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Ἀλέξανδρος</span></span><sup class="reference" id="ref_d_1"><a href="#cnote_d">[d]</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Dynasty" title="Dynasty">Dynasty</a></th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Argead_dynasty" title="Argead dynasty">Argead</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Father</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II of Macedon</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Mother</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Olympias" title="Olympias">Olympias of Epirus</a></td></tr><tr><th class="infobox-label" scope="row">Religion</th><td class="infobox-data"><a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_religion" title="Ancient Greek religion">Ancient Greek religion</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Alexander III of Macedon</b> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%88%CE%BB%CE%AD%CE%BE%CE%B1%CE%BD%CE%B4%CF%81%CE%BF%CF%82" title="wikt:Ἀλέξανδρος">Ἀλέξανδρος</a></span>, <small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Ancient_Greek" title="Romanization of Ancient Greek">romanized</a>: </small><span title="Ancient Greek-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">Alexandros</i></span>; 20/21 July 356 BC – 10/11 June 323 BC), commonly known as <b>Alexander the Great</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plautus_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plautus-2">[a]</a></sup> was a king of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greek</a> kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">Macedon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="ref_a_1"><a href="#cnote_a">[a]</a></sup> He succeeded his father <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II</a> to the throne in 336 BC at the age of 20, and spent most of his ruling years conducting a lengthy <a href="/wiki/Military_campaign" title="Military campaign">military campaign</a> throughout <a href="/wiki/Western_Asia" title="Western Asia">Western Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egypt</a>. By the age of thirty, he had created one of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_largest_empires" title="List of largest empires">largest empires</a> in history, stretching from <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> to northwestern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Historical_India" title="Historical India">India</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[2]</a></sup> He was undefeated in battle and is widely considered to be one of history's greatest and most successful military commanders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYenne2010159_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYenne2010159-4">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup>
</p><p>Until the age of 16, Alexander was tutored by <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>. In 335 BC, shortly after his assumption of kingship over Macedon, he <a href="/wiki/Alexander%27s_Balkan_campaign" title="Alexander's Balkan campaign">campaigned in the Balkans</a> and reasserted control over <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a> and <a href="/wiki/Illyria" title="Illyria">Illyria</a> before marching on the city of <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Greece" title="Thebes, Greece">Thebes</a>, which was <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Thebes" title="Battle of Thebes">subsequently destroyed in battle</a>. Alexander then led the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Corinth" title="League of Corinth">League of Corinth</a>, and used his authority to launch the <a href="/wiki/Greek_nationalism#History" title="Greek nationalism">pan-Hellenic project</a> envisaged by his father, assuming leadership over all <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greeks</a> in their conquest of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Iran" title="Greater Iran">Persia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeckelTritle200999_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeckelTritle200999-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 334 BC, he invaded the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Persian Empire</a> and began <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Alexander_the_Great#Persia" title="Wars of Alexander the Great">a series of campaigns</a> that lasted for 10 years. Following his conquest of <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Asia Minor</a>, Alexander broke the power of Achaemenid Persia in a series of decisive battles, including those at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Issus" title="Battle of Issus">Issus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gaugamela" title="Battle of Gaugamela">Gaugamela</a>; he subsequently overthrew <a href="/wiki/Darius_III" title="Darius III">Darius III</a> and conquered the Achaemenid Empire in its entirety.<sup class="reference" id="ref_b_1"><a href="#cnote_b">[b]</a></sup> After the fall of Persia, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empire_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Empire of Alexander the Great">Macedonian Empire</a> held a vast swath of territory between the <a href="/wiki/Adriatic_Sea" title="Adriatic Sea">Adriatic Sea</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus River</a>. Alexander endeavored to reach the "ends of the world and the Great Outer Sea" and <a href="/wiki/Indian_campaign_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Indian campaign of Alexander the Great">invaded India</a> in 326 BC, achieving an important victory over <a href="/wiki/Porus" title="Porus">Porus</a>, an ancient Indian king of present-day <a href="/wiki/Punjab" title="Punjab">Punjab</a>, at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Hydaspes" title="Battle of the Hydaspes">Battle of the Hydaspes</a>. Due to the demand of his homesick troops, he eventually turned back at the <a href="/wiki/Beas_River" title="Beas River">Beas River</a> and later died in 323 BC in <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>, the city of <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> that he had planned to establish as his empire's capital. <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Death of Alexander the Great">Alexander's death</a> left unexecuted an additional series of planned military and mercantile campaigns that would have begun with a Greek invasion of <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabia</a>. In the years following his death, <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Diadochi" title="Wars of the Diadochi">a series of civil wars</a> broke out across the Macedonian Empire, eventually leading to its disintegration at the hands of the <a href="/wiki/Diadochi" title="Diadochi">Diadochi</a>.
</p><p>With his death marking the start of the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a>, Alexander's legacy includes the cultural diffusion and syncretism that his conquests engendered, such as <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhism" title="Greco-Buddhism">Greco-Buddhism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_Judaism" title="Hellenistic Judaism">Hellenistic Judaism</a>. He founded more than <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_founded_by_Alexander_the_Great" title="List of cities founded by Alexander the Great">twenty cities that bore his name</a>, with the most prominent being the city of <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> in Egypt. Alexander's settlement of <a href="/wiki/Greek_colonisation" title="Greek colonisation">Greek colonists</a> and the resulting spread of <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Greece" title="Culture of Greece">Greek culture</a> led to the overwhelming dominance of Hellenistic civilization and influence as far east as the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>. The Hellenistic period developed through the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> into modern <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western culture</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek language</a> became the <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> of the region and was the predominant language of the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> up until its collapse in the mid-15th century AD. Greek-speaking communities <a href="/wiki/Cappadocian_Greeks" title="Cappadocian Greeks">in central Anatolia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pontic_Greeks" title="Pontic Greeks">in far-eastern Anatolia</a> survived until the <a href="/wiki/Greek_genocide" title="Greek genocide">Greek genocide</a> of the 1910s and early 1920s as well as the <a href="/wiki/Convention_Concerning_the_Exchange_of_Greek_and_Turkish_Populations" title="Convention Concerning the Exchange of Greek and Turkish Populations">Greek–Turkish population exchange</a> of the mid-1920s. Alexander became legendary as a classical hero in the mould of <a href="/wiki/Achilles" title="Achilles">Achilles</a>, featuring prominently in the historical and mythical traditions of both Greek and non-Greek cultures. His military achievements and unprecedented enduring successes in battle made him the measure against which many later military leaders would compare themselves,<sup class="reference" id="ref_c_1"><a href="#cnote_c">[c]</a></sup> and his tactics remain a significant subject of study in <a href="/wiki/Military_academy" title="Military academy">military academies</a> worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYenne2010viii_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYenne2010viii-8">[7]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lineage_and_childhood">Lineage and childhood</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_Macedonia_336_BC-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="794" data-file-width="992" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Map_Macedonia_336_BC-en.svg/220px-Map_Macedonia_336_BC-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Map_Macedonia_336_BC-en.svg/330px-Map_Macedonia_336_BC-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Map_Macedonia_336_BC-en.svg/440px-Map_Macedonia_336_BC-en.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_Macedonia_336_BC-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">The Kingdom of Macedon</a> in 336 BC, birthplace of Alexander</div></div></div>
<p>Alexander III was born in <a href="/wiki/Pella" title="Pella">Pella</a>, the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">Kingdom of Macedon</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup> on the sixth day of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_calendars" title="Ancient Greek calendars">ancient Greek month</a> of <a href="/wiki/Attic_calendar" title="Attic calendar">Hekatombaion</a>, which probably corresponds to 20 July 356 BC (although the exact date is uncertain).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> He was the son of the erstwhile king of Macedon, <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II</a>, and his fourth wife, <a href="/wiki/Olympias" title="Olympias">Olympias</a> (daughter of <a href="/wiki/Neoptolemus_I_of_Epirus" title="Neoptolemus I of Epirus">Neoptolemus I</a>, king of <a href="/wiki/Epirus_(ancient_state)" title="Epirus (ancient state)">Epirus</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> Although Philip had seven or eight wives, Olympias was his principal wife for some time, likely because she gave birth to Alexander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010171_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010171-13">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:195px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coin_olympias_mus_theski.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="676" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Coin_olympias_mus_theski.JPG/193px-Coin_olympias_mus_theski.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Coin_olympias_mus_theski.JPG/289px-Coin_olympias_mus_theski.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Coin_olympias_mus_theski.JPG/385px-Coin_olympias_mus_theski.JPG 2x" width="193"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coin_olympias_mus_theski.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Roman medallion depicting <a href="/wiki/Olympias" title="Olympias">Olympias</a>, Alexander's mother</div></div></div>
<p>Several legends surround Alexander's birth and childhood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010188_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010188-14">[13]</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greek</a> biographer <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a>, on the eve of the consummation of her marriage to Philip, Olympias dreamed that her womb was struck by a thunderbolt that caused a flame to spread "far and wide" before dying away. Sometime after the wedding, Philip is said to have seen himself, in a dream, securing his wife's womb with a <a href="/wiki/Seal_(emblem)" title="Seal (emblem)">seal</a> engraved with a lion's image.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA2_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA2-15">[14]</a></sup> Plutarch offered a variety of interpretations for these dreams: that Olympias was pregnant before her marriage, indicated by the sealing of her womb; or that Alexander's father was <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a>. Ancient commentators were divided about whether the ambitious Olympias promulgated the story of Alexander's divine parentage, variously claiming that she had told Alexander, or that she dismissed the suggestion as impious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA2_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-PA2-15">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the day Alexander was born, Philip was preparing a <a href="/wiki/Siege" title="Siege">siege</a> on the city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potidea" title="Potidea">Potidea</a> on the peninsula of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chalcidice" title="Chalcidice">Chalcidice</a>. That same day, Philip received news that his general <a href="/wiki/Parmenion" title="Parmenion">Parmenion</a> had defeated the combined <a href="/wiki/Illyria" title="Illyria">Illyrian</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paeonian" title="Paeonian">Paeonian</a> armies and that his horses had won at the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Olympic_Games" title="Ancient Olympic Games">Olympic Games</a>. It was also said that on this day, the <a href="/wiki/Temple_of_Artemis" title="Temple of Artemis">Temple of Artemis</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ephesus" title="Ephesus">Ephesus</a>, one of the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Wonders_of_the_Ancient_World" title="Seven Wonders of the Ancient World">Seven Wonders of the World</a>, burnt down. This led <a href="/wiki/Hegesias_of_Magnesia" title="Hegesias of Magnesia">Hegesias of Magnesia</a> to say that it had burnt down because <a href="/wiki/Artemis" title="Artemis">Artemis</a> was away, attending the birth of Alexander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup> Such legends may have emerged when Alexander was king, and possibly at his instigation, to show that he was superhuman and destined for greatness from conception.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010188_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010188-14">[13]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Archaeological_Site_of_Pella_by_Joy_of_Museums.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2741" data-file-width="5999" decoding="async" height="101" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Archaeological_Site_of_Pella_by_Joy_of_Museums.jpg/220px-Archaeological_Site_of_Pella_by_Joy_of_Museums.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Archaeological_Site_of_Pella_by_Joy_of_Museums.jpg/330px-Archaeological_Site_of_Pella_by_Joy_of_Museums.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Archaeological_Site_of_Pella_by_Joy_of_Museums.jpg/440px-Archaeological_Site_of_Pella_by_Joy_of_Museums.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Archaeological_Site_of_Pella_by_Joy_of_Museums.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Archaeological Site of <a href="/wiki/Pella" title="Pella">Pella</a>, Greece, Alexander's birthplace</div></div></div>
<p>In his early years, Alexander was raised by a nurse, <a href="/wiki/Lanike" title="Lanike">Lanike</a>, sister of Alexander's future general <a href="/wiki/Cleitus_the_Black" title="Cleitus the Black">Cleitus the Black</a>. Later in his childhood, Alexander was tutored by the strict <a href="/wiki/Leonidas_of_Epirus" title="Leonidas of Epirus">Leonidas</a>, a relative of his mother, and by <a href="/wiki/Lysimachus_of_Acarnania" title="Lysimachus of Acarnania">Lysimachus of Acarnania</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERenault200133–34_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERenault200133–34-17">[16]</a></sup> Alexander was raised in the manner of noble Macedonian youths, learning to read, play the <a href="/wiki/Lyre" title="Lyre">lyre</a>, ride, fight, and hunt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186-18">[17]</a></sup>
When Alexander was ten years old, a trader from <a href="/wiki/Thessaly" title="Thessaly">Thessaly</a> brought Philip a horse, which he offered to sell for thirteen <a href="/wiki/Attic_talent" title="Attic talent">talents</a>. The horse refused to be mounted, and Philip ordered it away. Alexander, however, detecting the horse's fear of its own shadow, asked to tame the horse, which he eventually managed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010188_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010188-14">[13]</a></sup> Plutarch stated that Philip, overjoyed at this display of courage and ambition, kissed his son tearfully, declaring: "My boy, you must find a kingdom big enough for your ambitions. Macedon is too small for you", and bought the horse for him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA6_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA6-19">[18]</a></sup> Alexander named it <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bucephalas" title="Bucephalas">Bucephalas</a>, meaning "ox-head". Bucephalas carried Alexander as far as <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>. When the animal died (because of old age, according to Plutarch, at age thirty), Alexander named a city after him, <a href="/wiki/Alexandria_Bucephalous" title="Alexandria Bucephalous">Bucephala</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alejandro_Magno,_Alexander_The_Great_Bust_Alexander_BM_1857_(cropped).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1949" data-file-width="1747" decoding="async" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Alejandro_Magno%2C_Alexander_The_Great_Bust_Alexander_BM_1857_%28cropped%29.jpg/170px-Alejandro_Magno%2C_Alexander_The_Great_Bust_Alexander_BM_1857_%28cropped%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Alejandro_Magno%2C_Alexander_The_Great_Bust_Alexander_BM_1857_%28cropped%29.jpg/255px-Alejandro_Magno%2C_Alexander_The_Great_Bust_Alexander_BM_1857_%28cropped%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Alejandro_Magno%2C_Alexander_The_Great_Bust_Alexander_BM_1857_%28cropped%29.jpg/340px-Alejandro_Magno%2C_Alexander_The_Great_Bust_Alexander_BM_1857_%28cropped%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alejandro_Magno,_Alexander_The_Great_Bust_Alexander_BM_1857_(cropped).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_art" title="Hellenistic art">Hellenistic</a> bust of a young Alexander the Great, possibly from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Egypt" title="Ptolemaic Egypt">Ptolemaic Egypt</a>, 2nd-1st century BC, now in the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a></div></div></div>
<p>When Alexander was 13, Philip began to search for a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tutor" title="Tutor">tutor</a>, and considered such academics as <a href="/wiki/Isocrates" title="Isocrates">Isocrates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Speusippus" title="Speusippus">Speusippus</a>, the latter offering to resign from his stewardship of the <a href="/wiki/Platonic_Academy" title="Platonic Academy">Academy</a> to take up the post. In the end, Philip chose <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> and provided the Temple of the Nymphs at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mieza,_Macedonia" title="Mieza, Macedonia">Mieza</a> as a classroom. In return for teaching Alexander, Philip agreed to rebuild Aristotle's hometown of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stageira" title="Stageira">Stageira</a>, which Philip had razed, and to repopulate it by buying and freeing the ex-citizens who were slaves, or pardoning those who were in exile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mieza was like a boarding school for Alexander and the children of Macedonian nobles, such as <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_I_Soter" title="Ptolemy I Soter">Ptolemy</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hephaistion" title="Hephaistion">Hephaistion</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cassander" title="Cassander">Cassander</a>. Many of these students would become his friends and future generals, and are often known as the "Companions". Aristotle taught Alexander and his companions about medicine, philosophy, morals, religion, logic, and art. Under Aristotle's tutelage, Alexander developed a passion for the works of <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>, and in particular the <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i>; Aristotle gave him an annotated copy, which Alexander later carried on his campaigns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alexander was able to quote <a href="/wiki/Euripides" title="Euripides">Euripides</a> from memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RLF_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-RLF-23">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>During his youth, Alexander was also acquainted with Persian exiles at the Macedonian court, who received the protection of Philip II for several years as they opposed <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_III" title="Artaxerxes III">Artaxerxes III</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECawthorne2004[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidoxyz0v9T74sCpgPA42_42–43]_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECawthorne2004[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidoxyz0v9T74sCpgPA42_42–43]-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EDC_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-EDC-26">[25]</a></sup> Among them were <a href="/wiki/Artabazos_II" title="Artabazos II">Artabazos II</a> and his daughter <a href="/wiki/Barsine" title="Barsine">Barsine</a>, possible future mistress of Alexander, who resided at the Macedonian court from 352 to 342 BC, as well as <a href="/wiki/Amminapes" title="Amminapes">Amminapes</a>, future <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satrap</a> of Alexander, or a Persian nobleman named <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sisines" title="Sisines">Sisines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECawthorne2004[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidoxyz0v9T74sCpgPA42_42–43]_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECawthorne2004[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidoxyz0v9T74sCpgPA42_42–43]-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JM_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-JM-27">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EJ_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-EJ-29">[28]</a></sup> This gave the Macedonian court a good knowledge of Persian issues, and may even have influenced some of the innovations in the management of the Macedonian state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JM_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-JM-27">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Suda" title="Suda">Suda</a> writes that <a href="/wiki/Anaximenes_of_Lampsacus" title="Anaximenes of Lampsacus">Anaximenes of Lampsacus</a> was one of Alexander's teachers, and that Anaximenes also accompanied Alexander on his campaigns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[29]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Heir_of_Philip_II">Heir of Philip II</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Regency_and_ascent_of_Macedon">Regency and ascent of Macedon</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II of Macedon</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rise_of_Macedon" title="Rise of Macedon">Rise of Macedon</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="History of Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">History of Macedonia (ancient kingdom)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Philip_II_of_Macedon_Ny_Carlsberg_Glyptotek_IN2263.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6000" data-file-width="4500" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Philip_II_of_Macedon_Ny_Carlsberg_Glyptotek_IN2263.jpg/170px-Philip_II_of_Macedon_Ny_Carlsberg_Glyptotek_IN2263.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Philip_II_of_Macedon_Ny_Carlsberg_Glyptotek_IN2263.jpg/255px-Philip_II_of_Macedon_Ny_Carlsberg_Glyptotek_IN2263.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Philip_II_of_Macedon_Ny_Carlsberg_Glyptotek_IN2263.jpg/340px-Philip_II_of_Macedon_Ny_Carlsberg_Glyptotek_IN2263.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Philip_II_of_Macedon_Ny_Carlsberg_Glyptotek_IN2263.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II of Macedon</a>, Alexander's father</div></div></div>
<p>At the age of 16, Alexander's education under Aristotle ended. Philip II had waged war against the <a href="/wiki/Thracians" title="Thracians">Thracians</a> to the north, which left Alexander in charge as <a href="/wiki/Regent" title="Regent">regent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heir_apparent" title="Heir apparent">heir apparent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010188_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010188-14">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>During Philip's absence, the <a href="/wiki/Thracians" title="Thracians">Thracian</a> tribe of <a href="/wiki/Maedi" title="Maedi">Maedi</a> revolted against Macedonia. Alexander responded quickly and drove them from their territory. The territory was colonized, and a city, named <a href="/wiki/Alexandropolis_Maedica" title="Alexandropolis Maedica">Alexandropolis</a>, was founded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>Upon Philip's return, Alexander was dispatched with a small force to subdue the revolts in southern <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a>. Campaigning against the Greek city of <a href="/wiki/Perinthus" title="Perinthus">Perinthus</a>, Alexander reportedly saved his father's life. Meanwhile, the city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphissa_(city)" title="Amphissa (city)">Amphissa</a> began to work lands that were sacred to <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a> near <a href="/wiki/Delphi" title="Delphi">Delphi</a>, a sacrilege that gave Philip the opportunity to further intervene in Greek affairs. While Philip was occupied in Thrace, Alexander was ordered to muster an army for a campaign in southern Greece. Concerned that other Greek states might intervene, Alexander made it look as though he was preparing to attack <a href="/wiki/Illyria" title="Illyria">Illyria</a> instead. During this turmoil, the Illyrians invaded Macedonia, only to be repelled by Alexander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERenault200147–49_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERenault200147–49-32">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Philip and his army joined his son in 338 BC, and they marched south through <a href="/wiki/Thermopylae" title="Thermopylae">Thermopylae</a>, taking it after stubborn resistance from its Theban garrison. They went on to occupy the city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elatea" title="Elatea">Elatea</a>, only a few days' march from both <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Greece" title="Thebes, Greece">Thebes</a>. The Athenians, led by <a href="/wiki/Demosthenes" title="Demosthenes">Demosthenes</a>, voted to seek alliance with Thebes against Macedonia. Both Athens and Philip sent embassies to win Thebes's favour, but Athens won the contest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> Philip marched on Amphissa (ostensibly acting on the request of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amphictyonic_League" title="Amphictyonic League">Amphictyonic League</a>), capturing the mercenaries sent there by Demosthenes and accepting the city's surrender. Philip then returned to Elatea, sending a final offer of peace to Athens and Thebes, who both rejected it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[33]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Chaeronea,_338_BC.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="531" data-file-width="752" decoding="async" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Battle_of_Chaeronea%2C_338_BC.png/220px-Battle_of_Chaeronea%2C_338_BC.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Battle_of_Chaeronea%2C_338_BC.png/330px-Battle_of_Chaeronea%2C_338_BC.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Battle_of_Chaeronea%2C_338_BC.png/440px-Battle_of_Chaeronea%2C_338_BC.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Chaeronea,_338_BC.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Battle plan from the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chaeronea_(338_BC)" title="Battle of Chaeronea (338 BC)">Battle of Chaeronea</a></div></div></div>
<p>As Philip marched south, his opponents blocked him near <a href="/wiki/Chaeronea" title="Chaeronea">Chaeronea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boeotia" title="Boeotia">Boeotia</a>. During the ensuing <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chaeronea_(338_BC)" title="Battle of Chaeronea (338 BC)">Battle of Chaeronea</a>, Philip commanded the right wing and Alexander the left, accompanied by a group of Philip's trusted generals. According to the ancient sources, the two sides fought bitterly for some time. Philip deliberately commanded his troops to retreat, counting on the untested Athenian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hoplites" title="Hoplites">hoplites</a> to follow, thus breaking their line. Alexander was the first to break the Theban lines, followed by Philip's generals. Having damaged the enemy's cohesion, Philip ordered his troops to press forward and quickly routed them. With the Athenians lost, the Thebans were surrounded. Left to fight alone, they were defeated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DiodXVI_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-DiodXVI-35">[34]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the victory at Chaeronea, Philip and Alexander marched unopposed into the Peloponnese, welcomed by all cities; however, when they reached <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a>, they were refused, but did not resort to war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[35]</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Corinth" title="Ancient Corinth">Corinth</a>, Philip established a "Hellenic Alliance" (modelled on the old <a href="/wiki/Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece#Hellenic_alliance" title="Second Persian invasion of Greece">anti-Persian alliance</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars" title="Greco-Persian Wars">Greco-Persian Wars</a>), which included most Greek city-states except Sparta. Philip was then named <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hegemon" title="Hegemon">Hegemon</a></i> (often translated as "Supreme Commander") of this league (known by modern scholars as the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Corinth" title="League of Corinth">League of Corinth</a>), and announced his plans to attack the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Persian Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERenault200154_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERenault200154-37">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCarty200426_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCarty200426-38">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exile_and_return">Exile and return</span></h3>
<p>When Philip returned to Pella, he fell in love with and married <a href="/wiki/Cleopatra_Eurydice_of_Macedon" title="Cleopatra Eurydice of Macedon">Cleopatra Eurydice</a> in 338 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup> the niece of his general <a href="/wiki/Attalus_(general)" title="Attalus (general)">Attalus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010179_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010179-40">[39]</a></sup> The marriage made Alexander's position as heir less secure, since any son of Cleopatra Eurydice would be a fully Macedonian heir, while Alexander was only half-Macedonian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCarty200427_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCarty200427-41">[40]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Banquet" title="Banquet">wedding banquet</a>, a drunken Attalus publicly prayed to the gods that the union would produce a legitimate heir.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010179_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010179-40">[39]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>At the wedding of Cleopatra, whom Philip fell in love with and married, she being much too young for him, her uncle Attalus in his drink desired the Macedonians would implore the gods to give them a lawful successor to the kingdom by his niece. This so irritated Alexander, that throwing one of the cups at his head, "You villain," said he, "what, am I then a bastard?" Then Philip, taking Attalus's part, rose up and would have run his son through; but by good fortune for them both, either his over-hasty rage, or the wine he had drunk, made his foot slip, so that he fell down on the floor. At which Alexander reproachfully insulted over him: "See there," said he, "the man who makes preparations to pass out of Europe into Asia, overturned in passing from one seat to another."</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Plutarch, describing the feud at Philip's wedding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA9_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA9-42">[41]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>In 337 BC, Alexander fled Macedon with his mother, dropping her off with her brother, King <a href="/wiki/Alexander_I_of_Epirus" title="Alexander I of Epirus">Alexander I of Epirus</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dodona" title="Dodona">Dodona</a>, capital of the <a href="/wiki/Molossians" title="Molossians">Molossians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010180_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010180-43">[42]</a></sup> He continued to Illyria,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010180_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010180-43">[42]</a></sup> where he sought refuge with one or more Illyrian kings, perhaps with <a href="/wiki/Glaucias_of_Taulantii" title="Glaucias of Taulantii">Glaukias</a>, and was treated as a guest, despite having defeated them in battle a few years before.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> However, it appears Philip never intended to disown his politically and militarily trained son.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010180_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010180-43">[42]</a></sup> Accordingly, Alexander returned to Macedon after six months due to the efforts of a family friend, <a href="/wiki/Demaratus_(hetairos)" title="Demaratus (hetairos)">Demaratus</a>, who mediated between the two parties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the following year, the Persian <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satrap</a> (governor) of <a href="/wiki/Caria" title="Caria">Caria</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pixodarus_of_Caria" title="Pixodarus of Caria">Pixodarus</a>, offered his eldest daughter to Alexander's half-brother, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philip_Arrhidaeus" title="Philip Arrhidaeus">Philip Arrhidaeus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010180_43-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010180-43">[42]</a></sup> Olympias and several of Alexander's friends suggested this showed Philip intended to make Arrhidaeus his heir.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010180_43-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010180-43">[42]</a></sup> Alexander reacted by sending an actor, <a href="/wiki/Thessalus_(actor)" title="Thessalus (actor)">Thessalus</a> of Corinth, to tell Pixodarus that he should not offer his daughter's hand to an illegitimate son, but instead to Alexander. When Philip heard of this, he stopped the negotiations and scolded Alexander for wishing to marry the daughter of a Carian, explaining that he wanted a better bride for him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010180_43-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010180-43">[42]</a></sup> Philip exiled four of Alexander's friends, <a href="/wiki/Harpalus" title="Harpalus">Harpalus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nearchus" title="Nearchus">Nearchus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_I_Soter" title="Ptolemy I Soter">Ptolemy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Erigyius" title="Erigyius">Erigyius</a>, and had the Corinthians bring Thessalus to him in chains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[45]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="King_of_Macedon">King of Macedon</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accession">Accession</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Government of Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">Government of Macedonia (ancient kingdom)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pausanius_assassinates_Philip_during_the_procession_into_the_theatre_by_Andre_Castaigne_(1898-1899).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2670" data-file-width="1638" decoding="async" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Pausanius_assassinates_Philip_during_the_procession_into_the_theatre_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29.jpg/170px-Pausanius_assassinates_Philip_during_the_procession_into_the_theatre_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Pausanius_assassinates_Philip_during_the_procession_into_the_theatre_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29.jpg/255px-Pausanius_assassinates_Philip_during_the_procession_into_the_theatre_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Pausanius_assassinates_Philip_during_the_procession_into_the_theatre_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29.jpg/340px-Pausanius_assassinates_Philip_during_the_procession_into_the_theatre_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pausanius_assassinates_Philip_during_the_procession_into_the_theatre_by_Andre_Castaigne_(1898-1899).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pausanius assassinates Philip II, Alexander's father, during his procession into the theatre</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Deer_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="462" decoding="async" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Deer_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg/220px-Deer_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Deer_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg/330px-Deer_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Deer_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg/440px-Deer_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Deer_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The emblema of the <a href="/wiki/Stag_Hunt_Mosaic" title="Stag Hunt Mosaic">Stag Hunt Mosaic</a>, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 300 BC</span>, from <a href="/wiki/Pella" title="Pella">Pella</a>; the figure on the right is possibly Alexander the Great due to the date of the mosaic along with the depicted upsweep of his centrally-parted hair (<i>anastole</i>); the figure on the left wielding a double-edged axe (associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hephaistos" title="Hephaistos">Hephaistos</a>) is perhaps <a href="/wiki/Hephaestion" title="Hephaestion">Hephaestion</a>, one of Alexander's loyal companions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bare_url_a_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-bare_url_a-47">[46]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In summer 336 BC, while at <a href="/wiki/Vergina" title="Vergina">Aegae</a> attending the wedding of his daughter <a href="/wiki/Cleopatra_of_Macedon" title="Cleopatra of Macedon">Cleopatra</a> to Olympias's brother, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_I_of_Epirus" title="Alexander I of Epirus">Alexander I of Epirus</a>, Philip was assassinated by the captain of his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Somatophylax" title="Somatophylax">bodyguards</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pausanias_of_Orestis" title="Pausanias of Orestis">Pausanias</a>.<sup class="reference" id="ref_e_1"><a href="#cnote_e">[e]</a></sup> As Pausanias tried to escape, he tripped over a vine and was killed by his pursuers, including two of Alexander's companions, <a href="/wiki/Perdiccas" title="Perdiccas">Perdiccas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leonnatus" title="Leonnatus">Leonnatus</a>. Alexander was proclaimed king on the spot by the nobles and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Macedonian_military" title="Ancient Macedonian military">army</a> at the age of 20.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCarty200430–31_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCarty200430–31-48">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fox_1980_72_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fox_1980_72-50">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consolidation_of_power">Consolidation of power</span></h3>
<p>Alexander began his reign by eliminating potential rivals to the throne. He had his cousin, the former <a href="/wiki/Amyntas_IV_of_Macedon" title="Amyntas IV of Macedon">Amyntas IV</a>, executed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010190_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010190-51">[50]</a></sup> He also had two Macedonian princes from the region of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lyncestis" title="Lyncestis">Lyncestis</a> killed, but spared a third, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexander_Lyncestes" title="Alexander Lyncestes">Alexander Lyncestes</a>. Olympias had Cleopatra Eurydice and Europa, her daughter by Philip, burned alive. When Alexander learned about this, he was furious. Alexander also ordered the murder of Attalus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010190_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010190-51">[50]</a></sup> who was in command of the advance guard of the army in Asia Minor and Cleopatra's uncle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green_2007_5–6_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Green_2007_5–6-52">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Attalus was at that time corresponding with Demosthenes, regarding the possibility of defecting to Athens. Attalus also had severely insulted Alexander, and following Cleopatra's murder, Alexander may have considered him too dangerous to leave alive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Green_2007_5–6_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Green_2007_5–6-52">[51]</a></sup> Alexander spared Arrhidaeus, who was by all accounts mentally disabled, possibly as a result of poisoning by Olympias.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCarty200430–31_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCarty200430–31-48">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fox_1980_72_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fox_1980_72-50">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>News of Philip's death roused many states into revolt, including Thebes, Athens, Thessaly, and the Thracian tribes north of Macedon. When news of the revolts reached Alexander, he responded quickly. Though advised to use diplomacy, Alexander mustered 3,000 Macedonian cavalry and rode south towards Thessaly. He found the Thessalian army occupying the pass between <a href="/wiki/Mount_Olympus" title="Mount Olympus">Mount Olympus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mount_Ossa_(Greece)" title="Mount Ossa (Greece)">Mount Ossa</a>, and ordered his men to ride over Mount Ossa. When the Thessalians awoke the next day, they found Alexander in their rear and promptly surrendered, adding their cavalry to Alexander's force. He then continued south towards the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnese" title="Peloponnese">Peloponnese</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alexander stopped at Thermopylae, where he was recognized as the leader of the Amphictyonic League before heading south to <a href="/wiki/Corinth" title="Corinth">Corinth</a>. Athens sued for peace and Alexander pardoned the rebels. The famous <a href="/wiki/Diogenes_and_Alexander" title="Diogenes and Alexander">encounter between Alexander and Diogenes the Cynic</a> occurred during Alexander's stay in Corinth. When Alexander asked Diogenes what he could do for him, the philosopher disdainfully asked Alexander to stand a little to the side, as he was blocking the sunlight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoneman200421_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoneman200421-55">[54]</a></sup> This reply apparently delighted Alexander, who is reported to have said "But verily, if I were not Alexander, I would like to be Diogenes."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDillon2004187–88_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDillon2004187–88-56">[55]</a></sup> At Corinth, Alexander took the title of <i>Hegemon</i> ("leader") and, like Philip, was appointed commander for the coming war against Persia. He also received news of a Thracian uprising.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Balkan_campaign">Balkan campaign</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alexander%27s_Balkan_campaign" title="Alexander's Balkan campaign">Alexander's Balkan campaign</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Macedonian_phalanx_counter-attacks_during_the_battle_of_the_carts.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1601" data-file-width="2309" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/The_Macedonian_phalanx_counter-attacks_during_the_battle_of_the_carts.jpg/220px-The_Macedonian_phalanx_counter-attacks_during_the_battle_of_the_carts.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/The_Macedonian_phalanx_counter-attacks_during_the_battle_of_the_carts.jpg/330px-The_Macedonian_phalanx_counter-attacks_during_the_battle_of_the_carts.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/The_Macedonian_phalanx_counter-attacks_during_the_battle_of_the_carts.jpg/440px-The_Macedonian_phalanx_counter-attacks_during_the_battle_of_the_carts.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Macedonian_phalanx_counter-attacks_during_the_battle_of_the_carts.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Macedonian phalanx at the "Battle of the Carts" against the Thracians in 335 BC</div></div></div>
<p>Before crossing to Asia, Alexander wanted to safeguard his northern borders. In the spring of 335 BC, he advanced to suppress several revolts. Starting from <a href="/wiki/Amphipolis" title="Amphipolis">Amphipolis</a>, he travelled east into the country of the "Independent Thracians"; and at <a href="/wiki/Haemus_Mons" title="Haemus Mons">Mount Haemus</a>, the Macedonian army attacked and defeated the Thracian forces manning the heights.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-I,_1_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-I,_1-58">[57]</a></sup> The Macedonians marched into the country of the <a href="/wiki/Triballi" title="Triballi">Triballi</a>, and defeated their army near the Lyginus river<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup> (a <a href="/wiki/List_of_tributaries_of_the_Danube" title="List of tributaries of the Danube">tributary of the Danube</a>). Alexander then marched for three days to the <a href="/wiki/Danube" title="Danube">Danube</a>, encountering the <a href="/wiki/Getae" title="Getae">Getae</a> tribe on the opposite shore. Crossing the river at night, he surprised them and forced their army to retreat after the first cavalry <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skirmish" title="Skirmish">skirmish</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>News then reached Alexander that the <a href="/wiki/Illyrians" title="Illyrians">Illyrian</a> chieftain <a href="/wiki/Cleitus_(son_of_Bardylis)" title="Cleitus (son of Bardylis)">Cleitus</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_Glaukias" title="King Glaukias">King Glaukias</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Taulantii" title="Taulantii">Taulantii</a> were in open revolt against his authority. Marching west into Illyria, Alexander defeated each in turn, forcing the two rulers to flee with their troops. With these victories, he secured his northern frontier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>While Alexander campaigned north, the Thebans and Athenians rebelled once again. Alexander immediately headed south.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010192_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010192-62">[61]</a></sup> While the other cities again hesitated, Thebes decided to fight. The Theban resistance was ineffective, and Alexander <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Thebes" title="Battle of Thebes">razed the city</a> and divided its territory between the other Boeotian cities. The end of Thebes cowed Athens, leaving all of Greece temporarily at peace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010192_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010192-62">[61]</a></sup> Alexander then set out on his Asian campaign, leaving <a href="/wiki/Antipater" title="Antipater">Antipater</a> as regent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_199_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_199-63">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conquest_of_the_Achaemenid_Persian_Empire">Conquest of the Achaemenid Persian Empire</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Wars of Alexander the Great">Wars of Alexander the Great</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_the_expedition_of_Alexander_the_Great_into_Asia" title="Chronology of the expedition of Alexander the Great into Asia">Chronology of the expedition of Alexander the Great into Asia</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asia_Minor">Asia Minor</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MacedonEmpire.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="951" data-file-width="1990" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/MacedonEmpire.jpg/330px-MacedonEmpire.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/MacedonEmpire.jpg/495px-MacedonEmpire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/MacedonEmpire.jpg/660px-MacedonEmpire.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MacedonEmpire.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map of Alexander's empire and his route</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright">

<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Granicus" title="Battle of the Granicus">Battle of the Granicus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Halicarnassus" title="Siege of Halicarnassus">Siege of Halicarnassus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Miletus" title="Siege of Miletus">Siege of Miletus</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:305px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Audran.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="792" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Audran.jpg/303px-Audran.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Audran.jpg/455px-Audran.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Audran.jpg/606px-Audran.jpg 2x" width="303"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Audran.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gérard Audran after Charles LeBrun, <a class="external text" href="https://library.nga.gov/discovery/fulldisplay?docid=alma991694413804896&amp;vid=01NGA_INST:IMAGE" rel="nofollow">'Alexander Entering Babylon,'</a> original print first published 1675, engraving, <a class="external text" href="https://www.nga.gov/research/library/imagecollections.html" rel="nofollow">Department of Image Collections, National Gallery of Art Library, Washington, DC.</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_cuts_the_Gordian_Knot.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1601" data-file-width="2048" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Alexander_cuts_the_Gordian_Knot.jpg/280px-Alexander_cuts_the_Gordian_Knot.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Alexander_cuts_the_Gordian_Knot.jpg/420px-Alexander_cuts_the_Gordian_Knot.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Alexander_cuts_the_Gordian_Knot.jpg/560px-Alexander_cuts_the_Gordian_Knot.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_cuts_the_Gordian_Knot.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Alexander Cuts the <a href="/wiki/Gordian_Knot" title="Gordian Knot">Gordian Knot</a></i> (1767) by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Simon_Berth%C3%A9lemy" title="Jean-Simon Berthélemy">Jean-Simon Berthélemy</a></div></div></div>
<p>After his victory at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chaeronea_(338_BC)" title="Battle of Chaeronea (338 BC)">Battle of Chaeronea (338 BC)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II</a> began the work of establishing himself as <i>hēgemṓn</i> (<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="el">ἡγεμών</span>) of a league which according to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diodorus" title="Diodorus">Diodorus</a> was to wage a campaign against the Persians for the sundry grievances Greece suffered in <a href="/wiki/Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="Second Persian invasion of Greece">480</a> and free the Greek cities of the western coast and islands from Achaemenid rule. In 336 he sent <a href="/wiki/Parmenion" title="Parmenion">Parmenion</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Amyntas_(son_of_Andromenes)" title="Amyntas (son of Andromenes)">Amyntas</a>, Andromenes and <a href="/wiki/Attalus_(general)" title="Attalus (general)">Attalus</a>, and an army of 10,000 men into <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> to make preparations for an invasion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PB_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-PB-64">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WH_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-WH-65">[64]</a></sup> At first, all went well. The Greek cities on the western coast of Anatolia revolted until the news arrived that Philip had been murdered and had been succeeded by his young son Alexander. The Macedonians were demoralized by Philip's death and were subsequently defeated near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnesia_on_the_Meander" title="Magnesia on the Meander">Magnesia</a> by the Achaemenids under the command of the mercenary <a href="/wiki/Memnon_of_Rhodes" title="Memnon of Rhodes">Memnon of Rhodes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PB_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-PB-64">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WH_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-WH-65">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Taking over the invasion project of Philip II, Alexander's army crossed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellespont" title="Hellespont">Hellespont</a> in 334 BC with approximately 48,100 soldiers, 6,100 cavalry and a fleet of 120 ships with crews numbering 38,000,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010192_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010192-62">[61]</a></sup> drawn from Macedon and various Greek city-states, mercenaries, and feudally raised soldiers from <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paionia" title="Paionia">Paionia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Illyria" title="Illyria">Illyria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="ref_f_1"><a href="#cnote_f">[f]</a></sup> He showed his intent to conquer the entirety of the Persian Empire by throwing a spear into Asian soil and saying he accepted Asia as a gift from the gods. This also showed Alexander's eagerness to fight, in contrast to his father's preference for diplomacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010192_62-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010192-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>After an initial victory against Persian forces at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Granicus" title="Battle of the Granicus">Battle of the Granicus</a>, Alexander accepted the surrender of the Persian provincial capital and treasury of <a href="/wiki/Sardis" title="Sardis">Sardis</a>; he then proceeded along the <a href="/wiki/Ionia" title="Ionia">Ionian</a> coast, granting autonomy and democracy to the cities. <a href="/wiki/Miletus" title="Miletus">Miletus</a>, held by Achaemenid forces, required a delicate siege operation, with Persian naval forces nearby. Further south, at <a href="/wiki/Halicarnassus" title="Halicarnassus">Halicarnassus</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Caria" title="Caria">Caria</a>, Alexander successfully waged his first large-scale <a href="/wiki/Siege" title="Siege">siege</a>, eventually forcing his opponents, the mercenary captain <a href="/wiki/Memnon_of_Rhodes" title="Memnon of Rhodes">Memnon of Rhodes</a> and the Persian <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satrap</a> of Caria, <a href="/wiki/Orontobates" title="Orontobates">Orontobates</a>, to withdraw by sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[66]</a></sup> Alexander left the government of Caria to a member of the Hecatomnid dynasty, <a href="/wiki/Ada_of_Caria" title="Ada of Caria">Ada</a>, who adopted Alexander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arrian_1976_loc=I,_23_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arrian_1976_loc=I,_23-68">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>From Halicarnassus, Alexander proceeded into mountainous <a href="/wiki/Lycia" title="Lycia">Lycia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pamphylia" title="Pamphylia">Pamphylian</a> plain, asserting control over all coastal cities to deny the Persians naval bases. From Pamphylia onwards the coast held no major ports and Alexander moved inland. At <a href="/wiki/Termessos" title="Termessos">Termessos</a>, Alexander humbled but did not storm the <a href="/wiki/Pisidia" title="Pisidia">Pisidian</a> city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup> At the ancient Phrygian capital of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gordium" title="Gordium">Gordium</a>, Alexander "undid" the hitherto unsolvable <a href="/wiki/Gordian_Knot" title="Gordian Knot">Gordian Knot</a>, a feat said to await the future "king of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup> According to the story, Alexander proclaimed that it did not matter how the knot was undone and hacked it apart with his sword.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Levant_and_Syria">The Levant and Syria</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Issus" title="Battle of Issus">Battle of Issus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Tyre_(332_BC)" title="Siege of Tyre (332 BC)">Siege of Tyre (332 BC)</a></div>
<p>In spring 333 BC, Alexander crossed the <a href="/wiki/Taurus_Mountains" title="Taurus Mountains">Taurus</a> into <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicia</a>. After a long pause due to an illness, he marched on towards Syria. Though outmanoeuvered by Darius's significantly larger army, he marched back to Cilicia, where he defeated Darius at <a href="/wiki/Issus_(Cilicia)" title="Issus (Cilicia)">Issus</a>. Darius fled the battle, causing his army to collapse, and left behind his wife, his two daughters, his mother <a href="/wiki/Sisygambis" title="Sisygambis">Sisygambis</a>, and a fabulous treasure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup> He offered a <a href="/wiki/Peace_treaty" title="Peace treaty">peace treaty</a> that included the lands he had already lost, and a ransom of 10,000 <a href="/wiki/Attic_talent" title="Attic talent">talents</a> for his family. Alexander replied that since he was now king of Asia, it was he alone who decided territorial divisions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[72]</a></sup>
Alexander proceeded to take possession of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Syria" title="Ancient Syria">Syria</a>, and most of the coast of the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arrian_1976_loc=I,_23_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-Arrian_1976_loc=I,_23-68">[67]</a></sup> In the following year, 332 BC, he was forced to attack <a href="/wiki/Tyre,_Lebanon" title="Tyre, Lebanon">Tyre</a>, which he captured after a long and difficult <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Tyre_(332_BC)" title="Siege of Tyre (332 BC)">siege</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[74]</a></sup> The men of military age were massacred and the women and children sold into <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Egypt">Egypt</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Gaza" title="Siege of Gaza">Siege of Gaza</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Name_of_Alexander_the_Great_in_Hieroglyphs_circa_330_BCE.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1964" data-file-width="2965" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Name_of_Alexander_the_Great_in_Hieroglyphs_circa_330_BCE.jpg/220px-Name_of_Alexander_the_Great_in_Hieroglyphs_circa_330_BCE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Name_of_Alexander_the_Great_in_Hieroglyphs_circa_330_BCE.jpg/330px-Name_of_Alexander_the_Great_in_Hieroglyphs_circa_330_BCE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Name_of_Alexander_the_Great_in_Hieroglyphs_circa_330_BCE.jpg/440px-Name_of_Alexander_the_Great_in_Hieroglyphs_circa_330_BCE.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Name_of_Alexander_the_Great_in_Hieroglyphs_circa_330_BCE.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Name of Alexander the Great in <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_hieroglyphs" title="Egyptian hieroglyphs">Egyptian hieroglyphs</a> (written from right to left), <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 332 BC</span>, Egypt. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louvre_Museum" title="Louvre Museum">Louvre Museum</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>When Alexander destroyed Tyre, most of the towns on the route to <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> quickly capitulated. However, Alexander was met with resistance at <a href="/wiki/Gaza_City" title="Gaza City">Gaza</a>. The stronghold was heavily fortified and built on a hill, requiring a siege. When "his engineers pointed out to him that because of the height of the mound it would be impossible... this encouraged Alexander all the more to make the
attempt".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup> After three unsuccessful assaults, the stronghold fell, but not before Alexander had received a serious shoulder wound. As in Tyre, men of military age were put to the sword and the women and children were sold into slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>Egypt was only one of a large number of territories taken by Alexander from the Persians. After his trip to Siwa, Alexander was crowned in the temple of Ptah at Memphis. It appears that the Egyptian people did not find it disturbing that he was a foreigner - nor that he was absent for virtually his entire reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-79">[78]</a></sup> Alexander restored the temples neglected by the Persians and dedicated new monuments to the Egyptian gods. In the temple of Luxor, near Karnak, he built a chapel for the sacred barge. During his brief months in Egypt, he reformed the taxation system on the Greek models and organized the military occupation of the country, but, early in 331 BC, he left for Asia in pursuit of the Persians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-79">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alexander advanced on Egypt in later 332 BC, where he was regarded as a liberator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup> To legitimize taking power and be recognized as the descendant of the long line of pharaohs, Alexander made sacrifices to the gods at Memphis and went to consult the famous oracle of Amun-Ra at the <a href="/wiki/Siwa_Oasis" title="Siwa Oasis">Siwa Oasis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-:0-79">[78]</a></sup> He was pronounced son of the deity <a href="/wiki/Amun" title="Amun">Amun</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Oracle" title="Oracle">Oracle</a> of <a href="/wiki/Siwa_Oasis" title="Siwa Oasis">Siwa Oasis</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Libya" title="Ancient Libya">Libyan</a> desert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBosworth198871–74_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBosworth198871–74-81">[80]</a></sup> Henceforth, Alexander often referred to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zeus-Ammon" title="Zeus-Ammon">Zeus-Ammon</a> as his true father, and after his death, currency depicted him adorned with the <a href="/wiki/Horns_of_Ammon" title="Horns of Ammon">Horns of Ammon</a> as a symbol of his divinity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup> The Greeks interpreted this message - one that the gods addressed to all pharaohs - as a prophecy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_79-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-79">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>During his stay in Egypt, he founded <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, which would become the prosperous capital of the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Kingdom" title="Ptolemaic Kingdom">Ptolemaic Kingdom</a> after his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup> Control of Egypt passed to Ptolemy I (son of Lagos), the founder of the Ptolemaic Dynasty (305-30 BC) after the death of Alexander.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Assyria_and_Babylonia">Assyria and Babylonia</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gaugamela" title="Battle of Gaugamela">Battle of Gaugamela</a></div>
<p>Leaving Egypt in 331 BC, Alexander marched eastward into <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Assyria" title="Achaemenid Assyria">Achaemenid Assyria</a> in <a href="/wiki/Upper_Mesopotamia" title="Upper Mesopotamia">Upper Mesopotamia</a> (now northern <a href="/wiki/Iraq" title="Iraq">Iraq</a>) and defeated Darius again at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Gaugamela" title="Battle of Gaugamela">Battle of Gaugamela</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> Darius once more fled the field, and Alexander chased him as far as <a href="/wiki/Erbil" title="Erbil">Arbela</a>. Gaugamela would be the final and decisive encounter between the two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> Darius fled over the mountains to <a href="/wiki/Ecbatana" title="Ecbatana">Ecbatana</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Hamadan" title="Hamadan">Hamadan</a>) while Alexander captured <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AIII16_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-AIII16-86">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Babylonian_astronomical_diaries" title="Babylonian astronomical diaries">Babylonian astronomical diaries</a> says that "the king of the world, Alexander" sends his scouts with a message to the people of Babylon before entering the city: "I shall not enter your houses".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Persia">Persia</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Persian_Gate" title="Battle of the Persian Gate">Battle of the Persian Gate</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:2persian_gate_wall.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="531" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/2persian_gate_wall.JPG/220px-2persian_gate_wall.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/2persian_gate_wall.JPG/330px-2persian_gate_wall.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/2persian_gate_wall.JPG/440px-2persian_gate_wall.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:2persian_gate_wall.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Site of the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gates" title="Persian Gates">Persian Gate</a> in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>; the road was built in the 1990s.</div></div></div>
<p>From Babylon, Alexander went to <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a>, one of the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid</a> capitals, and captured its treasury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AIII16_86-1"><a href="#cite_note-AIII16-86">[85]</a></sup> He sent the bulk of his army to the Persian ceremonial capital of <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a> via the Persian <a href="/wiki/Royal_Road" title="Royal Road">Royal Road</a>. Alexander himself took selected troops on the direct route to the city. He then stormed the pass of the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gates" title="Persian Gates">Persian Gates</a> (in the modern <a href="/wiki/Zagros_Mountains" title="Zagros Mountains">Zagros Mountains</a>) which had been blocked by a Persian army under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ariobarzanes_(satrap_of_Persis)" title="Ariobarzanes (satrap of Persis)">Ariobarzanes</a> and then hurried to Persepolis before its garrison could loot the treasury.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>On entering Persepolis, Alexander allowed his troops to loot the city for several days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup> Alexander stayed in Persepolis for five months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996121_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996121-90">[89]</a></sup> During his stay a fire broke out in the eastern palace of <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a> and spread to the rest of the city. Possible causes include a drunken accident or deliberate revenge for the burning of the <a href="/wiki/Acropolis_of_Athens" title="Acropolis of Athens">Acropolis of Athens</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars" title="Greco-Persian Wars">Second Persian War</a> by Xerxes;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHammond198372–73_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHammond198372–73-91">[90]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diodorus" title="Diodorus">Diodorus</a> allege that Alexander's companion, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hetaera" title="Hetaera">hetaera</a> <a href="/wiki/Tha%C3%AFs" title="Thaïs">Thaïs</a>, instigated and started the fire. Even as he watched the city burn, Alexander immediately began to regret his decision.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYenne2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidkngnd0GlUc4CpgPA99_99]_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYenne2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidkngnd0GlUc4CpgPA99_99]-92">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> claims that he ordered his men to put out the fires,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYenne2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidkngnd0GlUc4CpgPA99_99]_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYenne2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidkngnd0GlUc4CpgPA99_99]-92">[91]</a></sup> but that the flames had already spread to most of the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYenne2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidkngnd0GlUc4CpgPA99_99]_92-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYenne2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidkngnd0GlUc4CpgPA99_99]-92">[91]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Quintus_Curtius_Rufus" title="Quintus Curtius Rufus">Curtius</a> claims that Alexander did not regret his decision until the next morning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEYenne2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidkngnd0GlUc4CpgPA99_99]_92-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEYenne2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidkngnd0GlUc4CpgPA99_99]-92">[91]</a></sup> Plutarch recounts an anecdote in which Alexander pauses and talks to a fallen statue of Xerxes as if it were a live person:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Shall I pass by and leave you lying there because of the expeditions you led against Greece, or shall I set you up again because of your magnanimity and your virtues in other respects?<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fall_of_the_Persian_Empire_and_the_East">Fall of the Persian Empire and the East</span></h3>
