<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Khalili_Collection_Aramaic_Documents_manuscript_Bactria.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2500" data-file-width="4150" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Khalili_Collection_Aramaic_Documents_manuscript_Bactria.jpg/220px-Khalili_Collection_Aramaic_Documents_manuscript_Bactria.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Khalili_Collection_Aramaic_Documents_manuscript_Bactria.jpg/330px-Khalili_Collection_Aramaic_Documents_manuscript_Bactria.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Khalili_Collection_Aramaic_Documents_manuscript_Bactria.jpg/440px-Khalili_Collection_Aramaic_Documents_manuscript_Bactria.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Khalili_Collection_Aramaic_Documents_manuscript_Bactria.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Administrative document from <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a> dated to the seventh year of Alexander's reign (324 BC), bearing the first known use of the "Alexandros" form of his name, <a href="/wiki/Khalili_Collection_of_Aramaic_Documents" title="Khalili Collection of Aramaic Documents">Khalili Collection of Aramaic Documents</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Alexander then chased Darius, first into Media, and then Parthia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArrian1976III,_19–20_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArrian1976III,_19–20-97">[96]</a></sup> The Persian king no longer controlled his own destiny, and was taken prisoner by <a href="/wiki/Bessus" title="Bessus">Bessus</a>, his <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactrian</a> satrap and kinsman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArrian1976III,_21_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArrian1976III,_21-98">[97]</a></sup> As Alexander approached, Bessus had his men fatally stab the Great King and then declared himself Darius's successor as Artaxerxes V, before retreating into Central Asia to launch a <a href="/wiki/Guerrilla_warfare" title="Guerrilla warfare">guerrilla</a> campaign against Alexander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArrian1976III,_21,_25_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArrian1976III,_21,_25-99">[98]</a></sup> Alexander buried Darius's remains next to his Achaemenid predecessors in a regal funeral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArrian1976III,_22_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArrian1976III,_22-100">[99]</a></sup> He claimed that, while dying, Darius had named him as his successor to the Achaemenid throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGergel200481_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGergel200481-101">[100]</a></sup> The Achaemenid Empire is normally considered to have fallen with Darius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup> However, as basic forms of community life and the general structure of government were maintained and resuscitated by Alexander under his own rule, he, in the words of the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_studies" title="Iranian studies">Iranologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Briant" title="Pierre Briant">Pierre Briant</a> "may therefore be considered to have acted in many ways as the <i>last of the Achaemenids</i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant1985827–830_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant1985827–830-103">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alexander viewed Bessus as a usurper and set out to defeat him. This campaign, initially against Bessus, turned into a grand tour of central Asia. Alexander founded a series of new cities, all called Alexandria, including modern <a href="/wiki/Kandahar" title="Kandahar">Kandahar</a> in Afghanistan, and <a href="/wiki/Alexandria_Eschate" title="Alexandria Eschate">Alexandria Eschate</a> ("The Furthest") in modern <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>. The campaign took Alexander through <a href="/wiki/Media_(region)" title="Media (region)">Media</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aria_(satrapy)" title="Aria (satrapy)">Aria</a> (West Afghanistan), <a href="/wiki/Drangiana" title="Drangiana">Drangiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arachosia" title="Arachosia">Arachosia</a> (South and Central Afghanistan), <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a> (North and Central Afghanistan), and <a href="/wiki/Scythia" title="Scythia">Scythia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArrian1976III,_23–25,_27–30;_IV,_1–7_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArrian1976III,_23–25,_27–30;_IV,_1–7-104">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 329 BC, <a href="/wiki/Spitamenes" title="Spitamenes">Spitamenes</a>, who held an undefined position in the satrapy of Sogdiana, betrayed Bessus to <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_I_Soter" title="Ptolemy I Soter">Ptolemy</a>, one of Alexander's trusted companions, and Bessus was executed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArrian1976III,_30_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArrian1976III,_30-105">[104]</a></sup> However, when, at some point later, Alexander was on the <a href="/wiki/Syr_Darya" title="Syr Darya">Jaxartes</a> dealing with an incursion by a horse nomad army, Spitamenes raised Sogdiana in revolt. Alexander personally defeated the Scythians at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Jaxartes" title="Battle of Jaxartes">Battle of Jaxartes</a> and immediately launched a campaign against Spitamenes, defeating him in the Battle of Gabai. After the defeat, Spitamenes was killed by his own men, who then sued for peace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArrian1976IV,_5–6,_16–17_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArrian1976IV,_5–6,_16–17-106">[105]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Problems_and_plots">Problems and plots</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_killing_of_Cleitus_by_Andre_Castaigne_(1898-1899)_reduced.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2386" data-file-width="1537" decoding="async" height="342" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/The_killing_of_Cleitus_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29_reduced.jpg/220px-The_killing_of_Cleitus_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29_reduced.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/The_killing_of_Cleitus_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29_reduced.jpg/330px-The_killing_of_Cleitus_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29_reduced.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/The_killing_of_Cleitus_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29_reduced.jpg/440px-The_killing_of_Cleitus_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29_reduced.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_killing_of_Cleitus_by_Andre_Castaigne_(1898-1899)_reduced.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The Killing of <a href="/wiki/Cleitus_the_Black" title="Cleitus the Black">Cleitus</a></i>, by <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_Castaigne" title="André Castaigne">André Castaigne</a> (1898–1899)</div></div></div>
<p>During this time, Alexander adopted some elements of Persian dress and customs at his court, notably the custom of <i><a href="/wiki/Proskynesis" title="Proskynesis">proskynesis</a></i>, either a symbolic kissing of the hand, or prostration on the ground, that Persians showed to their social superiors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII11_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-AVII11-107">[106]</a></sup> This was one aspect of Alexander's broad strategy aimed at securing the aid and support of the Iranian upper classes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant1985827–830_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant1985827–830-103">[102]</a></sup> The Greeks however regarded the gesture of <i>proskynesis</i> as the province of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deities" title="Deities">deities</a> and believed that Alexander meant to deify himself by requiring it. This cost him the sympathies of many of his countrymen, and he eventually abandoned it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111-108">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the long rule of the Achaemenids, the elite positions in many segments of the empire including the central government, the army, and the many satrapies were specifically reserved for <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">Iranians</a> and to a major degree <a href="/wiki/Persians" title="Persians">Persian</a> noblemen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant1985827–830_103-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant1985827–830-103">[102]</a></sup> The latter were in many cases additionally connected through marriage alliances with the royal Achaemenid family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant1985827–830_103-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant1985827–830-103">[102]</a></sup> This created a problem for Alexander as to whether he had to make use of the various segments and people that had given the empire its solidity and unity for a lengthy period of time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant1985827–830_103-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant1985827–830-103">[102]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Briant" title="Pierre Briant">Pierre Briant</a> explains that Alexander realized that it was insufficient to merely exploit the internal contradictions within the imperial system as in Asia Minor, Babylonia or Egypt; he also had to (re)create a central government with or without the support of the Iranians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant1985827–830_103-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant1985827–830-103">[102]</a></sup> As early as 334 BC he demonstrated awareness of this, when he challenged incumbent King Darius III "by appropriating the main elements of the Achaemenid monarchy's ideology, particularly the theme of the king who protects the lands and the peasants".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant1985827–830_103-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant1985827–830-103">[102]</a></sup> Alexander wrote a letter in 332 BC to Darius III, wherein he argued that he was worthier than Darius "to succeed to the Achaemenid throne".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant1985827–830_103-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant1985827–830-103">[102]</a></sup> However, Alexander's eventual decision to burn the Achaemenid palace at <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a> in conjunction with the major rejection and opposition of the "entire Persian people" made it impracticable for him to pose himself as Darius' legitimate successor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant1985827–830_103-8"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant1985827–830-103">[102]</a></sup> Against Bessus (Artaxerxes V) however, Briant adds, Alexander reasserted "his claim to legitimacy as the avenger of Darius III".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant1985827–830_103-9"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant1985827–830-103">[102]</a></sup>
</p><p>A plot against his life was revealed, and one of his officers, <a href="/wiki/Philotas" title="Philotas">Philotas</a>, was executed for failing to alert Alexander. The death of the son necessitated the death of the father, and thus <a href="/wiki/Parmenion" title="Parmenion">Parmenion</a>, who had been charged with guarding the treasury at <a href="/wiki/Ecbatana" title="Ecbatana">Ecbatana</a>, was assassinated at Alexander's command, to prevent attempts at vengeance. Most infamously, Alexander personally killed the man who had saved his life at Granicus, <a href="/wiki/Cleitus_the_Black" title="Cleitus the Black">Cleitus the Black</a>, during a violent drunken altercation at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maracanda" title="Maracanda">Maracanda</a> (modern day <a href="/wiki/Samarkand" title="Samarkand">Samarkand</a> in <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>), in which Cleitus accused Alexander of several judgmental mistakes and most especially, of having forgotten the Macedonian ways in favour of a corrupt oriental lifestyle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGergel200499_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGergel200499-109">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>Later, in the Central Asian campaign, a second plot against his life was revealed, this one instigated by his own royal <a href="/wiki/Page_(servant)" title="Page (servant)">pages</a>. His official historian, <a href="/wiki/Callisthenes" title="Callisthenes">Callisthenes</a> of <a href="/wiki/Olynthus" title="Olynthus">Olynthus</a>, was implicated in the plot, and in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anabasis_of_Alexander" title="Anabasis of Alexander">Anabasis of Alexander</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Arrian" title="Arrian">Arrian</a> states that Callisthenes and the pages were then tortured on the <a href="/wiki/Rack_(torture)" title="Rack (torture)">rack</a> as punishment, and likely died soon after.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup> It remains unclear if Callisthenes was actually involved in the plot, for prior to his accusation he had fallen out of favour by leading the opposition to the attempt to introduce proskynesis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Macedon_in_Alexander.27s_absence"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Macedon_in_Alexander's_absence">Macedon in Alexander's absence</span></h3>
<p>When Alexander set out for Asia, he left his general <a href="/wiki/Antipater" title="Antipater">Antipater</a>, an experienced military and political leader and part of Philip II's "Old Guard", in charge of Macedon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_199_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_199-63">[62]</a></sup> Alexander's sacking of Thebes ensured that Greece remained quiet during his absence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_199_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_199-63">[62]</a></sup> The one exception was a call to arms by Spartan king <a href="/wiki/Agis_III" title="Agis III">Agis III</a> in 331 BC, whom Antipater defeated and killed in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Megalopolis" title="Battle of Megalopolis">battle of Megalopolis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_199_63-3"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_199-63">[62]</a></sup> Antipater referred the Spartans' punishment to the League of Corinth, which then deferred to Alexander, who chose to pardon them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup> There was also considerable friction between Antipater and Olympias, and each complained to Alexander about the other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>In general, Greece enjoyed a period of peace and prosperity during Alexander's campaign in Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup> Alexander sent back vast sums from his conquest, which stimulated the economy and increased trade across his empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> However, Alexander's constant demands for troops and the migration of Macedonians throughout his empire depleted Macedon's strength, greatly weakening it in the years after Alexander, and ultimately led to its subjugation by Rome after the <a href="/wiki/Third_Macedonian_War" title="Third Macedonian War">Third Macedonian War</a> (171–168 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coinage">Coinage</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silver_tetradrachm_of_Alexander_the_Great.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="319" data-file-width="681" decoding="async" height="103" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Silver_tetradrachm_of_Alexander_the_Great.jpg/220px-Silver_tetradrachm_of_Alexander_the_Great.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Silver_tetradrachm_of_Alexander_the_Great.jpg/330px-Silver_tetradrachm_of_Alexander_the_Great.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Silver_tetradrachm_of_Alexander_the_Great.jpg/440px-Silver_tetradrachm_of_Alexander_the_Great.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Silver_tetradrachm_of_Alexander_the_Great.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silver tetradrachm of Alexander the Great found in Byblos (ca 330-300 bc.)  (BnF 1998–859; 17,33g; Byblos, Price 3426b)</div></div></div>
<p>The conquest by <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pangaeum" title="Pangaeum">Pangaeum</a> and then of the island of <a href="/wiki/Thasos" title="Thasos">Thasos</a> between 356 and 342 BC brought rich gold and silver mines under Macedonian control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alexander appears to have introduced a new coinage in <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicia</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tarsus,_Mersin" title="Tarsus, Mersin">Tarsus</a>, after the Battle of Issus in 333 BC, which went on to become the main coinage of the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LE_RIDER_2003,_p153-214_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-LE_RIDER_2003,_p153-214-117">[116]</a></sup> Alexander minted gold <a href="/wiki/Stater" title="Stater">staters</a>, silver <a href="/wiki/Tetradrachm" title="Tetradrachm">tetradrachms</a> and drachmas, and bronze. The types of these coins remained constant in his empire. The gold series had the head of <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a> on the obverse and a winged Nike (Victory) on the reverse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup> The silver coinage had a beardless head of <a href="/wiki/Heracles" title="Heracles">Heracles</a> wearing a lionskin headdress on the obverse and <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a> aetophoros ('eagle bearer') enthroned with a scepter in his left hand, on the reverse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup> There are both Greek and non-Greek aspects to this design. Heracles and Zeus were important deities for the Macedonians, with Heracles was considered the ancestor of the Temenid dynasty and Zeus the patron of the main Macedonian sanctuary, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dium" title="Dium">Dium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LE_RIDER_2003,_p153-214_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-LE_RIDER_2003,_p153-214-117">[116]</a></sup> However, the lion was also the symbolic animal of the Anatolian god <a href="/wiki/Sandas" title="Sandas">Sandas</a>, worshipped at <a href="/wiki/Tarsus,_Mersin" title="Tarsus, Mersin">Tarsus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LE_RIDER_2003,_p153-214_117-2"><a href="#cite_note-LE_RIDER_2003,_p153-214-117">[116]</a></sup> The reverse design of Alexander's tetradrachms is closely modelled on the depiction of the god <a href="/wiki/Baaltars" title="Baaltars">Baaltars</a> (Baal of Tarsus), on the silver staters minted at Tarsus by the Persian satrap <a href="/wiki/Mazaeus" title="Mazaeus">Mazaeus</a> before Alexander's conquest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LE_RIDER_2003,_p153-214_117-3"><a href="#cite_note-LE_RIDER_2003,_p153-214-117">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alexander did not attempt to impose uniform imperial coinage throughout his new conquests. Persian coins continued to circulate in all the <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satrapies</a> of the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Indian_campaign">Indian campaign</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Indian_campaign_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Indian campaign of Alexander the Great">Indian campaign of Alexander the Great</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Forays_into_the_Indian_subcontinent">Forays into the Indian subcontinent</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_phalanx_attacking_the_centre_in_the_battle_of_the_Hydaspes_by_Andre_Castaigne_(1898-1899).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2244" data-file-width="1534" decoding="async" height="322" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/The_phalanx_attacking_the_centre_in_the_battle_of_the_Hydaspes_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29.jpg/220px-The_phalanx_attacking_the_centre_in_the_battle_of_the_Hydaspes_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/The_phalanx_attacking_the_centre_in_the_battle_of_the_Hydaspes_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29.jpg/330px-The_phalanx_attacking_the_centre_in_the_battle_of_the_Hydaspes_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/The_phalanx_attacking_the_centre_in_the_battle_of_the_Hydaspes_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29.jpg/440px-The_phalanx_attacking_the_centre_in_the_battle_of_the_Hydaspes_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_phalanx_attacking_the_centre_in_the_battle_of_the_Hydaspes_by_Andre_Castaigne_(1898-1899).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>The <a href="/wiki/Phalanx" title="Phalanx">Phalanx</a> Attacking the Centre in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Hydaspes" title="Battle of the Hydaspes">Battle of the Hydaspes</a></i> by André Castaigne (1898–1899)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AlexanderConquestsInIndia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1393" data-file-width="1117" decoding="async" height="274" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/AlexanderConquestsInIndia.jpg/220px-AlexanderConquestsInIndia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/AlexanderConquestsInIndia.jpg/330px-AlexanderConquestsInIndia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/AlexanderConquestsInIndia.jpg/440px-AlexanderConquestsInIndia.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AlexanderConquestsInIndia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alexander's invasion of the Indian subcontinent</div></div></div>
<p>After the death of <a href="/wiki/Spitamenes" title="Spitamenes">Spitamenes</a> and his marriage to Roxana (Raoxshna in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Iranian" title="Old Iranian">Old Iranian</a>) to cement relations with his new satrapies, Alexander turned to the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>. He invited the <a href="/wiki/Tribal_chief" title="Tribal chief">chieftains</a> of the former satrapy of <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandhara</a> (a region presently straddling eastern <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> and northern <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>), to come to him and submit to his authority. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Omphis" title="Omphis">Omphis</a> (Indian name <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambhi" title="Ambhi">Ambhi</a>), the ruler of <a href="/wiki/Taxila" title="Taxila">Taxila</a>, whose kingdom extended from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus" title="Indus">Indus</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Jhelum_River" title="Jhelum River">Hydaspes (Jhelum)</a>, complied, but the chieftains of some hill clans, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aspasioi" title="Aspasioi">Aspasioi</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assakenoi" title="Assakenoi">Assakenoi</a> sections of the <a href="/wiki/Kambojas" title="Kambojas">Kambojas</a> (known in Indian texts also as Ashvayanas and Ashvakayanas), refused to submit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETripathi1999118–21_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETripathi1999118–21-121">[120]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambhi" title="Ambhi">Ambhi</a> hastened to relieve Alexander of his apprehension and met him with valuable presents, placing himself and all his forces at his disposal. Alexander not only returned Ambhi his title and the gifts but he also presented him with a wardrobe of "Persian robes, gold and silver ornaments, 30 horses and 1,000 talents in gold". Alexander was emboldened to divide his forces, and Ambhi assisted <a href="/wiki/Hephaestion" title="Hephaestion">Hephaestion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Perdiccas" title="Perdiccas">Perdiccas</a> in constructing a bridge over the Indus where it bends at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hund,_Khyber_Pakhtunkhwa" title="Hund, Khyber Pakhtunkhwa">Hund</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup> supplied their troops with provisions, and received Alexander himself, and his whole army, in his capital city of Taxila, with every demonstration of friendship and the most liberal hospitality.
</p><p>On the subsequent advance of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">Macedonian</a> king, Taxiles accompanied him with a force of 5,000 men and took part in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Hydaspes_River" title="Battle of the Hydaspes River">battle of the Hydaspes River</a>. After that victory he was sent by Alexander in pursuit of <a href="/wiki/Porus" title="Porus">Porus</a>, to whom he was charged to offer favourable terms, but narrowly escaped losing his life at the hands of his old enemy. Subsequently, however, the two rivals were reconciled by the personal mediation of Alexander; and Taxiles, after having contributed zealously to the equipment of the fleet on the Hydaspes, was entrusted by the king with the government of the whole territory between that river and the Indus. A considerable accession of power was granted him after the death of <a href="/wiki/Philip_(son_of_Machatas)" title="Philip (son of Machatas)">Philip</a>, son of Machatas; and he was allowed to retain his authority at the death of Alexander himself (323 BC), as well as in the subsequent partition of the provinces at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Triparadisus" title="Treaty of Triparadisus">Triparadisus</a>, 321 BC.
</p><p>In the winter of 327/326 BC, Alexander personally led a campaign against the Aspasioi of <a href="/wiki/Kunar_Valley" title="Kunar Valley">Kunar</a> <a href="/wiki/Valley" title="Valley">valleys</a>, the Guraeans of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panjkora" title="Panjkora">Guraeus</a> valley, and the Assakenoi of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Swat_(Pakistan)" title="Swat (Pakistan)">Swat</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buner_Valley" title="Buner Valley">Buner</a> valleys.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup> A fierce contest ensued with the Aspasioi in which Alexander was wounded in the shoulder by a dart, but eventually the Aspasioi lost. Alexander then faced the Assakenoi, who fought against him from the strongholds of Massaga, Ora and <a href="/wiki/Aornos" title="Aornos">Aornos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETripathi1999118–21_121-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETripathi1999118–21-121">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>The fort of Massaga was reduced only after days of bloody fighting, in which Alexander was wounded seriously in the ankle. According to <a href="/wiki/Quintus_Curtius_Rufus" title="Quintus Curtius Rufus">Curtius</a>, "Not only did Alexander slaughter the entire population of Massaga, but also did he reduce its buildings to rubble."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[123]</a></sup> A similar slaughter followed at Ora. In the aftermath of Massaga and Ora, numerous Assakenians fled to the fortress of <a href="/wiki/Aornos" title="Aornos">Aornos</a>. Alexander followed close behind and captured the strategic hill-fort after four bloody days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETripathi1999118–21_121-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETripathi1999118–21-121">[120]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_accepts_the_surrender_of_Porus_by_Andre_Castaigne_(1898-1899).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1633" data-file-width="2380" decoding="async" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Alexander_accepts_the_surrender_of_Porus_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29.jpg/220px-Alexander_accepts_the_surrender_of_Porus_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Alexander_accepts_the_surrender_of_Porus_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29.jpg/330px-Alexander_accepts_the_surrender_of_Porus_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Alexander_accepts_the_surrender_of_Porus_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29.jpg/440px-Alexander_accepts_the_surrender_of_Porus_by_Andre_Castaigne_%281898-1899%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_accepts_the_surrender_of_Porus_by_Andre_Castaigne_(1898-1899).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/Porus" title="Porus">Porus</a> surrenders to Alexander</i></div></div></div>
<p>After Aornos, Alexander crossed the Indus and fought and won an epic battle against <a href="/wiki/Porus" title="Porus">King Porus</a>, who ruled a region lying between the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydaspes" title="Hydaspes">Hydaspes</a> and the Acesines (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chenab" title="Chenab">Chenab</a>), in what is now the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjab_region" title="Punjab region">Punjab</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Hydaspes" title="Battle of the Hydaspes">Battle of the Hydaspes</a> in 326 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETripathi1999124–25_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETripathi1999124–25-125">[124]</a></sup> Alexander was impressed by Porus's bravery, and made him an ally. He appointed Porus as satrap, and added to Porus's territory land that he did not previously own, towards the south-east, up to the Hyphasis (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beas" title="Beas">Beas</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-126">[125]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-127">[126]</a></sup> Choosing a local helped him control these lands so distant from Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETripathi1999126–27_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETripathi1999126–27-128">[127]</a></sup> Alexander founded two cities on opposite sides of the <a href="/wiki/Jhelum_River" title="Jhelum River">Hydaspes</a> river, naming one <a href="/wiki/Alexandria_Bucephalous" title="Alexandria Bucephalous">Bucephala</a>, in honour of his horse, who died around this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGergel2004120_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGergel2004120-129">[128]</a></sup> The other was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nicaea_(Punjab)" title="Nicaea (Punjab)">Nicaea</a> (Victory), thought to be located at the site of modern-day <a href="/wiki/Mong,_Punjab" title="Mong, Punjab">Mong, Punjab</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philostratus_the_Elder" title="Philostratus the Elder">Philostratus the Elder</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Life_of_Apollonius_of_Tyana" title="Life of Apollonius of Tyana">Life of Apollonius of Tyana</a> writes that in the army of Porus there was an elephant who fought brave against Alexander's army and Alexander dedicated it to the <a href="/wiki/Helios" title="Helios">Helios</a> (Sun) and named it Ajax, because he thought that a so great animal deserved a great name. The elephant had gold rings around its tusks and an inscription was on them written in Greek: "Alexander the son of Zeus dedicates Ajax to the Helios" (ΑΛΕΞΑΝΔΡΟΣ Ο ΔΙΟΣ ΤΟΝ ΑΙΑΝΤΑ ΤΩΙ ΗΛΙΩΙ).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Revolt_of_the_Hellenic_army">Revolt of the Hellenic army</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Asia_323bc.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1169" data-file-width="2040" decoding="async" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Asia_323bc.jpg/220px-Asia_323bc.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Asia_323bc.jpg/330px-Asia_323bc.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Asia_323bc.jpg/440px-Asia_323bc.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Asia_323bc.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Asia in 323 BC, the <a href="/wiki/Nanda_Empire" title="Nanda Empire">Nanda Empire</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gangaridai" title="Gangaridai">Gangaridai</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>, in relation to Alexander's Empire and neighbours</div></div></div>
<p>East of Porus's kingdom, near the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ganges_River" title="Ganges River">Ganges River</a>, was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nanda_dynasty" title="Nanda dynasty">Nanda Empire</a> of <a href="/wiki/Magadha" title="Magadha">Magadha</a>, and further east, the <a href="/wiki/Gangaridai" title="Gangaridai">Gangaridai Empire</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a> region of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>. Fearing the prospect of facing other large armies and exhausted by years of campaigning, Alexander's army mutinied at the <a href="/wiki/Beas_River" title="Beas River">Hyphasis River (Beas)</a>, refusing to march farther east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKosmin201434_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKosmin201434-132">[131]</a></sup> This river thus marks the easternmost extent of Alexander's conquests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETripathi1999129–30_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETripathi1999129–30-133">[132]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>As for the Macedonians, however, their struggle with Porus blunted their courage and stayed their further advance into India. For having had all they could do to repulse an enemy who mustered only twenty thousand infantry and two thousand horse, they violently opposed Alexander when he insisted on crossing the river Ganges also, the width of which, as they learned, was thirty-two furlongs [6.4 km], its depth one hundred fathoms [180 m], while its banks on the further side were covered with multitudes of <a href="/wiki/Man-at-arms" title="Man-at-arms">men-at-arms</a> and horsemen and elephants. For they were told that the kings of the Ganderites and Praesii were awaiting them with eighty thousand horsemen, two hundred thousand footmen, eight thousand chariots, and six thousand <a href="/wiki/War_elephant" title="War elephant">war elephants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA62_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA62-134">[133]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Alexander tried to persuade his soldiers to march farther, but his general <a href="/wiki/Coenus_(general)" title="Coenus (general)">Coenus</a> pleaded with him to change his opinion and return; the men, he said, "longed to again see their parents, their wives and children, their homeland". Alexander eventually agreed and turned south, marching along the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus</a>. Along the way his army conquered the <a href="/wiki/Malhi" title="Malhi">Malhi</a> (in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Multan" title="Multan">Multan</a>) and other Indian tribes and Alexander sustained an injury during the siege.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETripathi1999137–38_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETripathi1999137–38-135">[134]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alexander sent much of his army to <a href="/wiki/Kerman_province" title="Kerman province">Carmania</a> (modern southern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a>) with general <a href="/wiki/Craterus" title="Craterus">Craterus</a>, and commissioned a fleet to explore the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> shore under his admiral <a href="/wiki/Nearchus" title="Nearchus">Nearchus</a>, while he led the rest back to Persia through the more difficult southern route along the <a href="/wiki/Gedrosia" title="Gedrosia">Gedrosian Desert</a> and <a href="/wiki/Makran" title="Makran">Makran</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETripathi1999141_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETripathi1999141-136">[135]</a></sup> Alexander reached Susa in 324 BC, but not before losing many men to the harsh desert.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[136]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_years_in_Persia">Last years in Persia</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_and_Hephaestion.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Alexander_and_Hephaestion.jpg/220px-Alexander_and_Hephaestion.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Alexander_and_Hephaestion.jpg/330px-Alexander_and_Hephaestion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Alexander_and_Hephaestion.jpg/440px-Alexander_and_Hephaestion.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_and_Hephaestion.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alexander (left) and <a href="/wiki/Hephaestion" title="Hephaestion">Hephaestion</a> (right): Both were connected by a tight man-to-man friendship<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[137]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Discovering that many of his <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satraps</a> and military governors had misbehaved in his absence, Alexander executed several of them as examples on his way to <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVI27_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-AVI27-139">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII4_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-AVII4-140">[139]</a></sup> As a gesture of thanks, he paid off the debts of his soldiers, and announced that he would send over-aged and disabled veterans back to Macedon, led by Craterus. His troops misunderstood his intention and mutinied at the town of <a href="/wiki/Opis" title="Opis">Opis</a>. They refused to be sent away and criticized his adoption of Persian customs and dress and the introduction of Persian officers and soldiers into Macedonian units.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Worthington_2003_307_141-0"><a href="#cite_note-Worthington_2003_307-141">[140]</a></sup>
</p><p>After three days, unable to persuade his men to back down, Alexander gave Persians command posts in the army and conferred Macedonian military titles upon Persian units. The Macedonians quickly begged forgiveness, which Alexander accepted, and held a great banquet with several thousand of his men.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_194_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_194-142">[141]</a></sup> In an attempt to craft a lasting harmony between his Macedonian and Persian subjects, Alexander <a href="/wiki/Susa_weddings" title="Susa weddings">held a mass marriage</a> of his senior officers to Persian and other noblewomen at Susa, but few of those marriages seem to have lasted much beyond a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII4_140-1"><a href="#cite_note-AVII4-140">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Valenciennes,_Pierre-Henri_de_-_Alexander_at_the_Tomb_of_Cyrus_the_Great_-_1796.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="361" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Valenciennes%2C_Pierre-Henri_de_-_Alexander_at_the_Tomb_of_Cyrus_the_Great_-_1796.jpg/280px-Valenciennes%2C_Pierre-Henri_de_-_Alexander_at_the_Tomb_of_Cyrus_the_Great_-_1796.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Valenciennes%2C_Pierre-Henri_de_-_Alexander_at_the_Tomb_of_Cyrus_the_Great_-_1796.jpg/420px-Valenciennes%2C_Pierre-Henri_de_-_Alexander_at_the_Tomb_of_Cyrus_the_Great_-_1796.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Valenciennes%2C_Pierre-Henri_de_-_Alexander_at_the_Tomb_of_Cyrus_the_Great_-_1796.jpg/560px-Valenciennes%2C_Pierre-Henri_de_-_Alexander_at_the_Tomb_of_Cyrus_the_Great_-_1796.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Valenciennes,_Pierre-Henri_de_-_Alexander_at_the_Tomb_of_Cyrus_the_Great_-_1796.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Alexander at the Tomb of <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a></i>, by <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Henri_de_Valenciennes" title="Pierre-Henri de Valenciennes">Pierre-Henri de Valenciennes</a> (1796)</div></div></div>
<p>Meanwhile, upon his return to Persia, Alexander learned that guards of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tomb_of_Cyrus_the_Great" title="Tomb of Cyrus the Great">tomb of Cyrus the Great</a> in <a href="/wiki/Pasargadae" title="Pasargadae">Pasargadae</a> had desecrated it, and swiftly executed them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVI29_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-AVI29-143">[142]</a></sup> Alexander admired <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a>, from an early age reading Xenophon's <i><a href="/wiki/Cyropaedia" title="Cyropaedia">Cyropaedia</a></i>, which described Cyrus's heroism in battle and governance as a king and legislator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ulrich_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ulrich-144">[143]</a></sup> During his visit to Pasargadae, Alexander ordered his architect <a href="/wiki/Aristobulus_of_Cassandreia" title="Aristobulus of Cassandreia">Aristobulus</a> to decorate the interior of the sepulchral chamber of Cyrus's tomb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ulrich_144-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ulrich-144">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>Afterwards, Alexander travelled to Ecbatana to retrieve the bulk of the Persian treasure. There, his closest friend and possible lover, <a href="/wiki/Hephaestion" title="Hephaestion">Hephaestion</a>, died of illness or poisoning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII14_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-AVII14-145">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[145]</a></sup> Hephaestion's death devastated Alexander, and he ordered the preparation of an expensive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Funeral_pyre" title="Funeral pyre">funeral pyre</a> in Babylon, as well as a decree for public mourning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII14_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-AVII14-145">[144]</a></sup> Back in Babylon, Alexander planned a series of new campaigns, beginning with an invasion of Arabia, but he would not have a chance to realize them, as he died shortly after Hephaestion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII19_147-0"><a href="#cite_note-AVII19-147">[146]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_succession">Death and succession</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Death of Alexander the Great">Death of Alexander the Great</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Babylonian_astronomical_diary_recording_the_death_of_Alexander_the_Great_(British_Museum).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1704" data-file-width="2272" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Babylonian_astronomical_diary_recording_the_death_of_Alexander_the_Great_%28British_Museum%29.jpg/220px-Babylonian_astronomical_diary_recording_the_death_of_Alexander_the_Great_%28British_Museum%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Babylonian_astronomical_diary_recording_the_death_of_Alexander_the_Great_%28British_Museum%29.jpg/330px-Babylonian_astronomical_diary_recording_the_death_of_Alexander_the_Great_%28British_Museum%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Babylonian_astronomical_diary_recording_the_death_of_Alexander_the_Great_%28British_Museum%29.jpg/440px-Babylonian_astronomical_diary_recording_the_death_of_Alexander_the_Great_%28British_Museum%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Babylonian_astronomical_diary_recording_the_death_of_Alexander_the_Great_(British_Museum).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Babylonian_astronomical_diary" title="Babylonian astronomical diary">Babylonian astronomical diary</a> (c. 323–322 BC) recording the death of Alexander (<a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>, London)</div></div></div>
<p>On either 10 or 11 June 323 BC, Alexander died in the palace of <a href="/wiki/Nebuchadnezzar_II" title="Nebuchadnezzar II">Nebuchadnezzar II</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Babylon" title="Babylon">Babylon</a>, at age 32.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Depuydt_148-0"><a href="#cite_note-Depuydt-148">[147]</a></sup> There are two different versions of Alexander's death, and details of the death differ slightly in each. <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a>'s account is that roughly 14 days before his death, Alexander entertained admiral <a href="/wiki/Nearchus" title="Nearchus">Nearchus</a> and spent the night and next day drinking with <a href="/wiki/Medius_of_Larissa" title="Medius of Larissa">Medius of Larissa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P75_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-P75-149">[148]</a></sup> Alexander developed a fever, which worsened until he was unable to speak. The common soldiers, anxious about his health, were granted the right to file past him as he silently waved at them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWood20012267–70_150-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood20012267–70-150">[149]</a></sup> In the second account, <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus</a> recounts that Alexander was struck with pain after downing a large bowl of unmixed wine in honour of <a href="/wiki/Heracles" title="Heracles">Heracles</a>, followed by 11 days of weakness; he did not develop a fever, instead dying after some agony.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSXVII117_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-DSXVII117-151">[150]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Arrian" title="Arrian">Arrian</a> also mentioned this as an alternative, but Plutarch specifically denied this claim.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P75_149-1"><a href="#cite_note-P75-149">[148]</a></sup>
</p><p>Given the propensity of the Macedonian aristocracy to assassination,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen20071–2_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen20071–2-152">[151]</a></sup> foul play featured in multiple accounts of his death. Diodorus, Plutarch, Arrian and <a href="/wiki/Justin_(historian)" title="Justin (historian)">Justin</a> all mentioned the theory that Alexander was poisoned. Justin stated that Alexander was the victim of a poisoning conspiracy, Plutarch dismissed it as a fabrication,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA77_153-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA77-153">[152]</a></sup> while both Diodorus and Arrian noted that they mentioned it only for the sake of completeness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSXVII117_151-1"><a href="#cite_note-DSXVII117-151">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII27_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-AVII27-154">[153]</a></sup> The accounts were nevertheless fairly consistent in designating <a href="/wiki/Antipater" title="Antipater">Antipater</a>, recently removed as Macedonian viceroy, and at odds with Olympias, as the head of the alleged plot. Perhaps taking his summons to Babylon as a death sentence,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200723–24_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200723–24-155">[154]</a></sup> and having seen the fate of Parmenion and Philotas,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSXVII118_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-DSXVII118-156">[155]</a></sup> Antipater purportedly arranged for Alexander to be poisoned by his son Iollas, who was Alexander's wine-pourer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII27_154-1"><a href="#cite_note-AVII27-154">[153]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSXVII118_156-1"><a href="#cite_note-DSXVII118-156">[155]</a></sup> There was even a suggestion that Aristotle may have participated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII27_154-2"><a href="#cite_note-AVII27-154">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>The strongest argument against the poison theory is the fact that twelve days passed between the start of his illness and his death; such long-acting poisons were probably not available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELane_Fox2006chapter_32_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELane_Fox2006chapter_32-157">[156]</a></sup> However, in a 2003 BBC documentary investigating the death of Alexander, Leo Schep from the New Zealand National Poisons Centre proposed that the plant white hellebore (<i><a href="/wiki/Veratrum_album" title="Veratrum album">Veratrum album</a></i>), which was known in antiquity, may have been used to poison Alexander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[157]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECawthorne2004138_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECawthorne2004138-159">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[159]</a></sup> In a 2014 manuscript in the journal <i><a href="/wiki/Clinical_Toxicology" title="Clinical Toxicology">Clinical Toxicology</a></i>, Schep suggested Alexander's wine was spiked with <i>Veratrum album</i>, and that this would produce poisoning symptoms that match the course of events described in the <i><a href="/wiki/Alexander_Romance" title="Alexander Romance">Alexander Romance</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schep_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-schep-161">[160]</a></sup> <i>Veratrum album</i> poisoning can have a prolonged course and it was suggested that if Alexander was poisoned, <i>Veratrum album</i> offers the most plausible cause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schep_161-1"><a href="#cite_note-schep-161">[160]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[161]</a></sup> Another poisoning explanation put forward in 2010 proposed that the circumstances of his death were compatible with poisoning by water of the river Styx (modern-day <a href="/wiki/Mavroneri" title="Mavroneri">Mavroneri</a> in Arcadia, Greece) that contained <a href="/wiki/Calicheamicin" title="Calicheamicin">calicheamicin</a>, a dangerous compound produced by bacteria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[162]</a></sup>
</p><p>Several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Natural_causes" title="Natural causes">natural causes</a> (diseases) have been suggested, including <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Typhoid_fever" title="Typhoid fever">typhoid fever</a>. A 1998 article in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_England_Journal_of_Medicine" title="New England Journal of Medicine">New England Journal of Medicine</a></i> attributed his death to typhoid fever complicated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bowel_perforation" title="Bowel perforation">bowel perforation</a> and ascending <a href="/wiki/Paralysis" title="Paralysis">paralysis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMD_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMD-164">[163]</a></sup> Another recent analysis suggested pyogenic (infectious) <a href="/wiki/Spondylitis" title="Spondylitis">spondylitis</a> or <a href="/wiki/Meningitis" title="Meningitis">meningitis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ashrafian_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-ashrafian-165">[164]</a></sup> Other illnesses fit the symptoms, including <a href="/wiki/Acute_pancreatitis" title="Acute pancreatitis">acute pancreatitis</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Nile_virus" title="West Nile virus">West Nile virus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ref1_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-ref1-166">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[166]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Guillain-Barr%C3%A9_syndrome" title="Guillain-Barré syndrome">Guillain-Barré syndrome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[167]</a></sup> Natural-cause theories also tend to emphasize that Alexander's health may have been in general decline after years of heavy drinking and severe wounds. The anguish that Alexander felt after <a href="/wiki/Hephaestion" title="Hephaestion">Hephaestion</a>'s death may also have contributed to his declining health.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMD_164-1"><a href="#cite_note-AMD-164">[163]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-death_events">Post-death events</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Tomb_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Tomb of Alexander the Great">Tomb of Alexander the Great</a></div>
<p>Alexander's body was laid in a gold anthropoid <a href="/wiki/Sarcophagus" title="Sarcophagus">sarcophagus</a> that was filled with honey, which was in turn placed in a gold casket.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sarco1_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-sarco1-169">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[169]</a></sup> According to Aelian, a seer called Aristander foretold that the land where Alexander was laid to rest "would be happy and unvanquishable forever".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aelian_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aelian-171">[170]</a></sup> Perhaps more likely, the successors may have seen possession of the body as a symbol of legitimacy, since burying the prior king was a <a href="/wiki/Royal_prerogative" title="Royal prerogative">royal prerogative</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200732_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200732-172">[171]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mid-nineteenth_century_reconstruction_of_Alexander%27s_catafalque_based_on_the_description_by_Diodorus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1166" data-file-width="2977" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Mid-nineteenth_century_reconstruction_of_Alexander%27s_catafalque_based_on_the_description_by_Diodorus.jpg/280px-Mid-nineteenth_century_reconstruction_of_Alexander%27s_catafalque_based_on_the_description_by_Diodorus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Mid-nineteenth_century_reconstruction_of_Alexander%27s_catafalque_based_on_the_description_by_Diodorus.jpg/420px-Mid-nineteenth_century_reconstruction_of_Alexander%27s_catafalque_based_on_the_description_by_Diodorus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Mid-nineteenth_century_reconstruction_of_Alexander%27s_catafalque_based_on_the_description_by_Diodorus.jpg/560px-Mid-nineteenth_century_reconstruction_of_Alexander%27s_catafalque_based_on_the_description_by_Diodorus.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mid-nineteenth_century_reconstruction_of_Alexander%27s_catafalque_based_on_the_description_by_Diodorus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>19th-century depiction of Alexander's funeral procession, based on the description by <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus Siculus</a></div></div></div>
<p>While Alexander's funeral cortege was on its way to Macedon, Ptolemy seized it and took it temporarily to Memphis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sarco1_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-sarco1-169">[168]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aelian_171-1"><a href="#cite_note-Aelian-171">[170]</a></sup> His successor, <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_II_Philadelphus" title="Ptolemy II Philadelphus">Ptolemy II Philadelphus</a>, transferred the sarcophagus to Alexandria, where it remained until at least <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" title="Late Antiquity">late Antiquity</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ptolemy_IX_Lathyros" title="Ptolemy IX Lathyros">Ptolemy IX Lathyros</a>, one of Ptolemy's final successors, replaced Alexander's sarcophagus with a glass one so he could convert the original to coinage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sarco2_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-sarco2-173">[172]</a></sup> The recent discovery of an <a href="/wiki/Kasta_Tomb" title="Kasta Tomb">enormous tomb</a> in northern Greece, at <a href="/wiki/Amphipolis" title="Amphipolis">Amphipolis</a>, dating from the time of Alexander the Great<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174"><a href="#cite_note-174">[173]</a></sup> has given rise to speculation that its original intent was to be the burial place of Alexander. This would fit with the intended destination of Alexander's funeral cortege. However, the memorial was found to be dedicated to the dearest friend of Alexander the Great, <a href="/wiki/Hephaestion" title="Hephaestion">Hephaestion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[175]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Sarcophagus_Battle_of_Issus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2763" data-file-width="4911" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Alexander_Sarcophagus_Battle_of_Issus.jpg/220px-Alexander_Sarcophagus_Battle_of_Issus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Alexander_Sarcophagus_Battle_of_Issus.jpg/330px-Alexander_Sarcophagus_Battle_of_Issus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Alexander_Sarcophagus_Battle_of_Issus.jpg/440px-Alexander_Sarcophagus_Battle_of_Issus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_Sarcophagus_Battle_of_Issus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Detail of Alexander on the <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Sarcophagus" title="Alexander Sarcophagus">Alexander Sarcophagus</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pompey" title="Pompey">Pompey</a>, <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a> all visited the tomb in Alexandria, where Augustus, allegedly, accidentally knocked the nose off. <a href="/wiki/Caligula" title="Caligula">Caligula</a> was said to have taken Alexander's breastplate from the tomb for his own use. Around AD 200, Emperor <a href="/wiki/Septimius_Severus" title="Septimius Severus">Septimius Severus</a> closed Alexander's tomb to the public. His son and successor, <a href="/wiki/Caracalla" title="Caracalla">Caracalla</a>, a great admirer, visited the tomb during his own reign. After this, details on the fate of the tomb are hazy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sarco2_173-1"><a href="#cite_note-sarco2-173">[172]</a></sup>
</p><p>The so-called "<a href="/wiki/Alexander_Sarcophagus" title="Alexander Sarcophagus">Alexander Sarcophagus</a>", discovered near <a href="/wiki/Sidon" title="Sidon">Sidon</a> and now in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Istanbul_Archaeology_Museum" title="Istanbul Archaeology Museum">Istanbul Archaeology Museum</a>, is so named not because it was thought to have contained Alexander's remains, but because its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bas-relief" title="Bas-relief">bas-reliefs</a> depict Alexander and his companions fighting the Persians and hunting. It was originally thought to have been the sarcophagus of <a href="/wiki/Abdalonymus" title="Abdalonymus">Abdalonymus</a> (died 311 BC), the king of Sidon appointed by Alexander immediately following the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Issus" title="Battle of Issus">battle of Issus</a> in 331.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[177]</a></sup> However, more recently, it has been suggested that it may date from earlier than Abdalonymus's death.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Demades" title="Demades">Demades</a> likened the Macedonian army, after the death of Alexander, to the blinded <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyclops" title="Cyclops">Cyclops</a>, due to the many random and disorderly movements that it made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181"><a href="#cite_note-181">[180]</a></sup> In addition, Leosthenes, also, likened the anarchy between the generals, after Alexander's death, to the blinded Cyclops "who after he had lost his eye went feeling and groping about with his hands before him, not knowing where to lay them".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182"><a href="#cite_note-182">[181]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Division_of_the_Macedonian_Empire">Division of the Macedonian Empire</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Babylon" title="Partition of Babylon">Partition of Babylon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diadochi" title="Diadochi">Diadochi</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diadochi_EN.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="961" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Diadochi_EN.png/280px-Diadochi_EN.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Diadochi_EN.png/420px-Diadochi_EN.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Diadochi_EN.png/560px-Diadochi_EN.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diadochi_EN.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Kingdoms of the <a href="/wiki/Diadochi" title="Diadochi">Diadochi</a> in 301 BC: the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Kingdom" title="Ptolemaic Kingdom">Ptolemaic Kingdom</a> (dark blue), the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a> (yellow), <a href="/wiki/Pergamon" title="Pergamon">Kingdom of Pergamon</a> (orange), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Macedon" title="Kingdom of Macedon">Kingdom of Macedon</a> (green). Also shown are the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> (light blue), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carthaginian_Empire" title="Carthaginian Empire">Carthaginian Republic</a> (purple), and the <a href="/wiki/Epirus_(ancient_state)" title="Epirus (ancient state)">Kingdom of Epirus</a> (red).</div></div></div>
<p>Alexander's death was so sudden that when reports of his death reached Greece, they were not immediately believed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_199_63-4"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_199-63">[62]</a></sup> Alexander had no obvious or legitimate heir, his son Alexander IV by Roxane being born after Alexander's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200724–26_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200724–26-183">[182]</a></sup> According to Diodorus, Alexander's companions asked him on his deathbed to whom he bequeathed his kingdom; his laconic reply was "tôi kratistôi"—"to the strongest".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSXVII117_151-2"><a href="#cite_note-DSXVII117-151">[150]</a></sup> Another theory is that his successors wilfully or erroneously misheard "tôi Kraterôi"—"to Craterus", the general leading his Macedonian troops home and newly entrusted with the regency of Macedonia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shipley_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-Shipley-184">[183]</a></sup>
</p><p>Arrian and Plutarch claimed that Alexander was speechless by this point, implying that this was an apocryphal story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[184]</a></sup> Diodorus, Curtius and Justin offered the more plausible story that Alexander passed his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seal_(device)" title="Seal (device)">signet ring</a> to <a href="/wiki/Perdiccas" title="Perdiccas">Perdiccas</a>, a bodyguard and leader of the companion cavalry, in front of witnesses, thereby nominating him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSXVII117_151-3"><a href="#cite_note-DSXVII117-151">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200724–26_183-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200724–26-183">[182]</a></sup>
</p><p>Perdiccas initially did not claim power, instead suggesting that Roxane's baby would be king, if male; with himself, <a href="/wiki/Craterus" title="Craterus">Craterus</a>, Leonnatus, and Antipater as guardians. However, the infantry, under the command of <a href="/wiki/Meleager_(general)" title="Meleager (general)">Meleager</a>, rejected this arrangement since they had been excluded from the discussion. Instead, they supported Alexander's half-brother Philip Arrhidaeus. Eventually, the two sides reconciled, and after the birth of Alexander IV, he and Philip III were appointed joint kings, albeit in name only.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200726–29_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200726–29-186">[185]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dissension and rivalry soon affected the Macedonians, however. The satrapies handed out by Perdiccas at the <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Babylon" title="Partition of Babylon">Partition of Babylon</a> became power bases each general used to bid for power. After the assassination of Perdiccas in 321 BC, Macedonian unity collapsed, and 40 years of war between "The Successors" (<i><a href="/wiki/Diadochi" title="Diadochi">Diadochi</a></i>) ensued before the Hellenistic world settled into four stable power blocs: <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_Kingdom" title="Ptolemaic Kingdom">Ptolemaic Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Mesopotamia and Central Asia</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Attalid_dynasty" title="Attalid dynasty">Attalid Anatolia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Antigonid_dynasty" title="Antigonid dynasty">Antigonid Macedon</a>. In the process, both Alexander IV and Philip III were murdered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200729–34_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200729–34-187">[186]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_plans">Last plans</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KINGS_of_MACEDON_Alexander_III_the_Great_336-323_BC.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="798" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/KINGS_of_MACEDON_Alexander_III_the_Great_336-323_BC.jpg/330px-KINGS_of_MACEDON_Alexander_III_the_Great_336-323_BC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/KINGS_of_MACEDON_Alexander_III_the_Great_336-323_BC.jpg/495px-KINGS_of_MACEDON_Alexander_III_the_Great_336-323_BC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/KINGS_of_MACEDON_Alexander_III_the_Great_336-323_BC.jpg/660px-KINGS_of_MACEDON_Alexander_III_the_Great_336-323_BC.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KINGS_of_MACEDON_Alexander_III_the_Great_336-323_BC.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A coin of Alexander the Great struck by <a href="/wiki/Balakros" title="Balakros">Balakros</a> or his successor <a href="/wiki/Menes_of_Pella" title="Menes of Pella">Menes</a>, both former <i><a href="/wiki/Somatophylakes" title="Somatophylakes">somatophylakes</a></i> (bodyguards) of Alexander, when they held the position of <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satrap</a> of <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicia</a> in the lifetime of Alexander, <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 333-327 BC</span>. The obverse shows <a href="/wiki/Heracles" title="Heracles">Heracles</a>, ancestor of the Macedonian royal line and the reverse shows a seated Zeus Aëtophoros.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[187]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus</a> stated that Alexander had given detailed written instructions to <a href="/wiki/Craterus" title="Craterus">Craterus</a> some time before his death, which are known as Alexander's "last plans".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSXVIII4_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-DSXVIII4-189">[188]</a></sup> Craterus started to carry out Alexander's commands, but the successors chose not to further implement them, on the grounds they were impractical and extravagant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSXVIII4_189-1"><a href="#cite_note-DSXVIII4-189">[188]</a></sup> Furthermore, <a href="/wiki/Perdiccas" title="Perdiccas">Perdiccas</a> had read the notebooks containing Alexander's last plans to the Macedonian troops in Babylon, who voted not to carry them out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_199_63-5"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_199-63">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Diodorus, Alexander's last plans called for military expansion into the southern and western Mediterranean, monumental constructions, and the intermixing of Eastern and Western populations. It included:
</p>
<ul><li>Construction of 1,000 ships larger than triremes, along with harbours and a road running along the African coast all the way to the <a href="/wiki/Pillars_of_Hercules" title="Pillars of Hercules">Pillars of Hercules</a>, to be used for an invasion of Carthage and the western Mediterranean;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bad_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bad-190">[189]</a></sup></li>
<li>Erection of great temples in <a href="/wiki/Delos" title="Delos">Delos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Delphi" title="Delphi">Delphi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dodona" title="Dodona">Dodona</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dium" title="Dium">Dium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amphipolis" title="Amphipolis">Amphipolis</a>, all costing 1,500 <a href="/wiki/Attic_talent" title="Attic talent">talents</a>, and a monumental temple to <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a> at <a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">Troy</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_199_63-6"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_199-63">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bad_190-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bad-190">[189]</a></sup></li>
<li>Amalgamation of small settlements into larger cities ("<a href="/wiki/Synoecism" title="Synoecism">synoecisms</a>") and the "transplant of populations from Asia to Europe and in the opposite direction from Europe to Asia, in order to bring the largest continent to common unity and to friendship by means of intermarriage and family ties"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191"><a href="#cite_note-191">[190]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bad_190-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bad-190">[189]</a></sup></li>
<li>Construction of a monumental tomb for his father Philip, "to match the greatest of the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_pyramids" title="Egyptian pyramids">pyramids of Egypt</a>"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_199_63-7"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_199-63">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bad_190-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bad-190">[189]</a></sup></li>
<li>Conquest of Arabia<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_199_63-8"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_199-63">[62]</a></sup></li>
<li>Circumnavigation of Africa<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_199_63-9"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_199-63">[62]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>The enormous scale of these plans has led many scholars to doubt their historicity. <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Badian" title="Ernst Badian">Ernst Badian</a> argued that they were exaggerated by Perdiccas in order to ensure that the Macedonian troops voted not to carry them out.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bad_190-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bad-190">[189]</a></sup> Other scholars have proposed that they were invented by later authors within the tradition of the <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Romance" title="Alexander Romance">Alexander Romance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[191]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Character">Character</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Generalship">Generalship</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Military_tactics_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Military tactics of Alexander the Great">Military tactics of Alexander the Great</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Battle_granicus.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="573" data-file-width="746" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Battle_granicus.png/220px-Battle_granicus.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Battle_granicus.png/330px-Battle_granicus.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Battle_granicus.png/440px-Battle_granicus.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Battle_granicus.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Granicus" title="Battle of the Granicus">Battle of the Granicus</a>, 334 BC</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Battle_issus_decisive.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="563" data-file-width="738" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Battle_issus_decisive.png/220px-Battle_issus_decisive.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Battle_issus_decisive.png/330px-Battle_issus_decisive.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Battle_issus_decisive.png/440px-Battle_issus_decisive.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Battle_issus_decisive.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Issus" title="Battle of Issus">Battle of Issus</a>, 333 BC</div></div></div>
<p>Alexander perhaps earned the epithet "the Great" due to his unparalleled success as a military commander; he never lost a battle, despite typically being outnumbered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidlkYFVJ3U-BICpgPA192_192]_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidlkYFVJ3U-BICpgPA192_192]-193">[192]</a></sup> This was due to use of terrain, <a href="/wiki/Phalanx" title="Phalanx">phalanx</a> and cavalry tactics, bold strategy, and the fierce loyalty of his troops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_193_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_193-194">[193]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Macedonian_phalanx" title="Macedonian phalanx">Macedonian phalanx</a>, armed with the <a href="/wiki/Sarissa" title="Sarissa">sarissa</a>, a spear 6 metres (20 ft) long, had been developed and perfected by Philip II through rigorous training, and Alexander used its speed and manoeuvrability to great effect against larger but more disparate Persian forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996110_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996110-195">[194]</a></sup> Alexander also recognized the potential for disunity among his diverse army, which employed various languages and weapons. He overcame this by being personally involved in battle,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996121_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996121-90">[89]</a></sup> in the manner of a Macedonian king.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_193_194-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_193-194">[193]</a></sup>
</p><p>In his first battle in Asia, at Granicus, Alexander used only a small part of his forces, perhaps 13,000 infantry with 5,000 cavalry, against a much larger Persian force of 40,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196"><a href="#cite_note-196">[195]</a></sup> Alexander placed the phalanx at the center and cavalry and archers on the wings, so that his line matched the length of the Persian cavalry line, about 3 km (1.86 mi). By contrast, the Persian infantry was stationed behind its cavalry. This ensured that Alexander would not be outflanked, while his phalanx, armed with long pikes, had a considerable advantage over the Persians' <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scimitars" title="Scimitars">scimitars</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Javelins" title="Javelins">javelins</a>. Macedonian losses were negligible compared to those of the Persians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996122_197-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996122-197">[196]</a></sup>
</p><p>At Issus in 333 BC, his first confrontation with Darius, he used the same deployment, and again the central phalanx pushed through.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996122_197-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996122-197">[196]</a></sup> Alexander personally led the charge in the center, routing the opposing army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010193_198-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010193-198">[197]</a></sup> At the decisive encounter with Darius at Gaugamela, Darius equipped his chariots with scythes on the wheels to break up the phalanx and equipped his cavalry with pikes.
