Alexander arranged a double phalanx, with the center advancing at an angle, parting when the chariots bore down and then reforming. The advance was successful and broke Darius's center, causing the latter to flee once again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996122_197-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996122-197">[196]</a></sup>
</p><p>When faced with opponents who used unfamiliar fighting techniques, such as in Central Asia and India, Alexander adapted his forces to his opponents' style. Thus, in <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sogdiana" title="Sogdiana">Sogdiana</a>, Alexander successfully used his javelin throwers and archers to prevent outflanking movements, while massing his cavalry at the center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010193_198-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010193-198">[197]</a></sup> In India, confronted by Porus's elephant corps, the Macedonians opened their ranks to envelop the elephants and used their sarissas to strike upwards and dislodge the elephants' handlers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roisman_2010_194_142-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roisman_2010_194-142">[141]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_appearance">Physical appearance</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AlexanderCameo.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="975" data-file-width="932" decoding="async" height="230" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/AlexanderCameo.JPG/220px-AlexanderCameo.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/AlexanderCameo.JPG/330px-AlexanderCameo.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/AlexanderCameo.JPG/440px-AlexanderCameo.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AlexanderCameo.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alexander Cameo by <a href="/wiki/Pyrgoteles" title="Pyrgoteles">Pyrgoteles</a></div></div></div>
<p>Historical sources frequently give conflicting accounts of Alexander's appearance, and the earliest sources are the most scant in their detail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[198]</a></sup> During his lifetime, Alexander carefully curated his image by commissioning works from famous and great artists of the time. This included commissioning sculptures by <a href="/wiki/Lysippos" title="Lysippos">Lysippos</a>, paintings by <a href="/wiki/Apelles" title="Apelles">Apelles</a> and gem engravings by <a href="/wiki/Pyrgoteles" title="Pyrgoteles">Pyrgoteles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alexander_the_Great_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alexander_the_Great-200">[199]</a></sup> Ancient authors recorded that Alexander was so pleased with portraits of himself created by Lysippos that he forbade other sculptors from crafting his image; scholars today, however, find the claim dubious.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201"><a href="#cite_note-201">[200]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alexander_the_Great_200-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alexander_the_Great-200">[199]</a></sup> Nevertheless, Andrew Stewart highlights the fact that artistic portraits, not least because of who they are commissioned by, are always partisan, and that artistic portrayals of Alexander "seek to legitimize him (or, by extension, his Successors), to interpret him to their audiences, to answer their critiques, and to persuade them of his greatness", and thus should be considered within a framework of "praise and blame", in the same way sources such as praise poetry are.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[201]</a></sup> Despite those caveats, Lysippos's sculpture, famous for its naturalism, as opposed to a stiffer, more static pose, is thought to be the most faithful depiction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBosworth198819–20_203-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBosworth198819–20-203">[202]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Quintus_Curtius_Rufus" title="Quintus Curtius Rufus">Curtius Rufus</a>, a Roman historian from the first century AD, who wrote the <i><a href="/wiki/Histories_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Histories of Alexander the Great">Histories of Alexander the Great</a></i>, gives this account of Alexander sitting on the throne of <a href="/wiki/Darius_III" title="Darius III">Darius III</a>:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Then Alexander seating himself on the royal throne, which was far too high for his bodily stature. Therefore, since his feet did not reach its lowest step, once of the royal pages placed a table under his feet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERolfe19465.2.13_204-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERolfe19465.2.13-204">[203]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Both Curtius and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diodorus" title="Diodorus">Diodorus</a> report a story that when <a href="/wiki/Darius_III" title="Darius III">Darius III</a>'s mother, <a href="/wiki/Sisygambis" title="Sisygambis">Sisygambis</a>, first met Alexander and <a href="/wiki/Hephaestion" title="Hephaestion">Hephaestion</a>, she assumed that the latter was Alexander because he was the taller and more handsome of the two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[204]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander-lysippus1-1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="855" decoding="async" height="263" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Alexander-lysippus1-1.jpg/220px-Alexander-lysippus1-1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Alexander-lysippus1-1.jpg/330px-Alexander-lysippus1-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Alexander-lysippus1-1.jpg/440px-Alexander-lysippus1-1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander-lysippus1-1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alexander portrayal by <a href="/wiki/Lysippos" title="Lysippos">Lysippos</a></div></div></div>
<p>Details from the <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Sarcophagus" title="Alexander Sarcophagus">Alexander Sarcophagus</a> show that he had a fair complexion with ruddy cheeks. This is in line with the description of him given by the Greek biographer <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">  45</span> – c.<span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 120 AD</span>):
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The outward appearance of Alexander is best represented by the statues of him which Lysippus made, and it was by this artist alone that Alexander himself thought it fit that he should be modelled. For those peculiarities which many of his successors and friends afterwards tried to imitate, namely, the poise of the neck, which was bent slightly to the left, and the melting glance of his eyes, this artist has accurately observed. Apelles, however, in painting him as wielder of the thunder-bolt, did not reproduce his complexion, but made it too dark and swarthy. Whereas he was of a fair colour, as they say, and his fairness passed into ruddiness on his breast particularly, and in his face. Moreover, that a very pleasant odour exhaled from his skin and that there was a fragrance about his mouth and all his flesh, so that his garments were filled with it, this we have read in the <i>Memoirs of Aristoxenus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1919[httpswwwperseustuftseduhoppertextdocPerseustext1999010243chapter4highlightlysippus_IV,_1]_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1919[httpswwwperseustuftseduhoppertextdocPerseustext1999010243chapter4highlightlysippus_IV,_1]-206">[205]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Detail_of_fresco_depicting_Alexander_the_Great.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1028" data-file-width="823" decoding="async" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Detail_of_fresco_depicting_Alexander_the_Great.jpg/220px-Detail_of_fresco_depicting_Alexander_the_Great.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Detail_of_fresco_depicting_Alexander_the_Great.jpg/330px-Detail_of_fresco_depicting_Alexander_the_Great.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Detail_of_fresco_depicting_Alexander_the_Great.jpg/440px-Detail_of_fresco_depicting_Alexander_the_Great.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Detail_of_fresco_depicting_Alexander_the_Great.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A fresco depicting a hunt scene at the tomb of <a href="/wiki/Philip_II_of_Macedon" title="Philip II of Macedon">Philip II</a>, Alexander's father, at the Archaeological Site of <a href="/wiki/Aegae_(Macedonia)" title="Aegae (Macedonia)">Aigai</a>, the only known depiction of Alexander made during his lifetime, 330s BC</div></div></div>
<p>Historians have understood the detail of the pleasant odour attributed to Alexander as stemming from a belief in ancient Greece that pleasant scents are characteristic of gods and heroes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alexander_the_Great_200-2"><a href="#cite_note-Alexander_the_Great-200">[199]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Mosaic" title="Alexander Mosaic">Alexander Mosaic</a> and contemporary coins portray Alexander with "a straight nose, a slightly protruding jaw, full lips and eyes deep set beneath a strongly pronounced forehead".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alexander_the_Great_200-3"><a href="#cite_note-Alexander_the_Great-200">[199]</a></sup> The ancient historian <a href="/wiki/Claudius_Aelianus" title="Claudius Aelianus">Aelian</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 175</span> – <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 235 AD</span>), in his <i>Varia Historia</i> (12.14), describes Alexander's hair color as "<i>ξανθὴν</i>", that could mean yellowish, reddish or brownish.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiddellScott1940[httpswwwperseustuftseduhoppertextdocPerseustext1999040057entrycanqos_ξανθός]_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiddellScott1940[httpswwwperseustuftseduhoppertextdocPerseustext1999040057entrycanqos_ξανθός]-207">[206]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[207]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[208]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many scholars and historians attribute <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heterochromia" title="Heterochromia">heterochromia</a> to him. He is described as having one eye light and one eye dark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210"><a href="#cite_note-210">[209]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211"><a href="#cite_note-211">[210]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[211]</a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/The_Anabasis_of_Alexander" title="The Anabasis of Alexander">The Anabasis of Alexander</a></i>, Arrian is quoted as saying, "he had one eye dark as the night, and one blue as the sky".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[212]</a></sup> However, some have denied this claim as being used to emphasise the otherworldly and heroic qualities of Alexander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[213]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[214]</a></sup> Reconstruction of the original <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polychromy" title="Polychromy">polychromy</a> of the relief with Alexander on the <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Sarcophagus" title="Alexander Sarcophagus">sarcophagus</a> shows him with brown eyes and chestnut brown hair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[215]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personality">Personality</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lion_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="384" data-file-width="850" decoding="async" height="99" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Lion_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg/220px-Lion_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Lion_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg/330px-Lion_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/Lion_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg/440px-Lion_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lion_hunt_mosaic_from_Pella.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alexander (left), wearing a <i><a href="/wiki/Kausia" title="Kausia">kausia</a></i> and fighting an <a href="/wiki/Asiatic_lion" title="Asiatic lion">Asiatic lion</a> with his friend <a href="/wiki/Craterus" title="Craterus">Craterus</a> (detail); late 4th century BC <a href="/wiki/Mosaic" title="Mosaic">mosaic</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217"><a href="#cite_note-217">[216]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pella" title="Pella">Pella</a> Museum</div></div></div>
<p>Both of Alexander's parents encouraged his ambitions. His father Philip was probably Alexander's most immediate and influential role model, as the young Alexander watched him campaign practically every year, winning victory after victory while ignoring severe wounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010190_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010190-51">[50]</a></sup> Alexander's relationship with his father "forged" the competitive side of his personality; he had a need to outdo his father, illustrated by his reckless behavior in battle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200715–16_218-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200715–16-218">[217]</a></sup> While Alexander worried that his father would leave him "no great or brilliant achievement to be displayed to the world",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA5_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA5-219">[218]</a></sup> he also downplayed his father's achievements to his companions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200715–16_218-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200715–16-218">[217]</a></sup> Alexander's mother Olympia similarly had huge ambitions, and encouraged her son to believe it was his destiny to conquer the Persian Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200715–16_218-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200715–16-218">[217]</a></sup> She instilled a sense of destiny in him,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen20074_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen20074-220">[219]</a></sup> and Plutarch tells how his ambition "kept his spirit serious and lofty in advance of his years".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA4_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA4-221">[220]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Plutarch, Alexander also had a violent temper and rash, impulsive nature,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII29_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-AVII29-222">[221]</a></sup> and this could influence his decision making.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200715–16_218-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200715–16-218">[217]</a></sup> Although Alexander was stubborn and did not respond well to orders from his father, he was open to reasoned debate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA7_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA7-223">[222]</a></sup> He had a calmer side—perceptive, logical, and calculating. He had a great desire for knowledge, a love for philosophy, and was an avid reader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA8_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA8-224">[223]</a></sup> This was no doubt in part due to Aristotle's tutelage; Alexander was intelligent and quick to learn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200715–16_218-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200715–16-218">[217]</a></sup> His intelligent and rational side was amply demonstrated by his ability and success as a general.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII29_222-1"><a href="#cite_note-AVII29-222">[221]</a></sup> He had great self-restraint in "pleasures of the body", in contrast with his lack of <a href="/wiki/Self-control" title="Self-control">self-control</a> with alcohol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII28_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-AVII28-225">[224]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_the_Great_Ny_Carlsberg_Glyptotek_IN574_n1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5000" data-file-width="3750" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Alexander_the_Great_Ny_Carlsberg_Glyptotek_IN574_n1.jpg/170px-Alexander_the_Great_Ny_Carlsberg_Glyptotek_IN574_n1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Alexander_the_Great_Ny_Carlsberg_Glyptotek_IN574_n1.jpg/255px-Alexander_the_Great_Ny_Carlsberg_Glyptotek_IN574_n1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Alexander_the_Great_Ny_Carlsberg_Glyptotek_IN574_n1.jpg/340px-Alexander_the_Great_Ny_Carlsberg_Glyptotek_IN574_n1.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_the_Great_Ny_Carlsberg_Glyptotek_IN574_n1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Roman_sculpture" title="Roman sculpture">Roman copy</a> of an original 3rd century BC <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_sculpture" title="Ancient Greek sculpture">Greek bust</a> depicting Alexander the Great, <a href="/wiki/Ny_Carlsberg_Glyptotek" title="Ny Carlsberg Glyptotek">Ny Carlsberg Glyptotek</a>, Copenhagen</div></div></div>
<p>Alexander was erudite and patronized both arts and sciences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA4_221-1"><a href="#cite_note-PA4-221">[220]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA8_224-1"><a href="#cite_note-PA8-224">[223]</a></sup> However, he had little interest in sports or the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Olympic_Games" title="Ancient Olympic Games">Olympic Games</a> (unlike his father), seeking only the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homeric" title="Homeric">Homeric</a> ideals of honour (<i>timê</i>) and glory (<i>kudos</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226"><a href="#cite_note-226">[225]</a></sup> He had great <a href="/wiki/Charisma" title="Charisma">charisma</a> and force of personality, characteristics which made him a great leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200724–26_183-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200724–26-183">[182]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII29_222-2"><a href="#cite_note-AVII29-222">[221]</a></sup> His unique abilities were further demonstrated by the inability of any of his generals to unite Macedonia and retain the Empire after his death—only Alexander had the ability to do so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200724–26_183-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200724–26-183">[182]</a></sup>
</p><p>During his final years, and especially after the death of Hephaestion, Alexander began to exhibit signs of <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/megalomania" title="wikt:megalomania">megalomania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paranoia" title="Paranoia">paranoia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200723–24_155-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200723–24-155">[154]</a></sup> His extraordinary achievements, coupled with his own ineffable sense of destiny and the flattery of his companions, may have combined to produce this effect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200720–21_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200720–21-227">[226]</a></sup> His <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Delusions_of_grandeur" title="Delusions of grandeur">delusions of grandeur</a> are readily visible in his <a href="#Will">will</a> and in his desire to conquer the world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200723–24_155-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200723–24-155">[154]</a></sup> in as much as he is by various sources described as having <i>boundless ambition</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[227]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-229"><a href="#cite_note-229">[228]</a></sup> an epithet, the meaning of which has descended into a historical cliché.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-230"><a href="#cite_note-230">[229]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-231"><a href="#cite_note-231">[230]</a></sup>
</p><p>He appears to have believed himself a deity, or at least sought to deify himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200723–24_155-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200723–24-155">[154]</a></sup> Olympias always insisted to him that he was the son of Zeus,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA3_232-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA3-232">[231]</a></sup> a theory apparently confirmed to him by the oracle of Amun at <a href="/wiki/Siwa_Oasis" title="Siwa Oasis">Siwa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P27_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-P27-233">[232]</a></sup> He began to identify himself as the son of Zeus-Ammon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P27_233-1"><a href="#cite_note-P27-233">[232]</a></sup> Alexander adopted elements of Persian dress and customs at court, notably <i><a href="/wiki/Proskynesis" title="Proskynesis">proskynesis</a></i>, which was one aspect of Alexander's broad strategy aimed at securing the aid and support of the Iranian upper classes;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBriant1985827–830_103-10"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBriant1985827–830-103">[102]</a></sup> however the practise of <i>proskynesis</i> was disapproved by the Macedonians, and they were unwilling to perform it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII11_107-1"><a href="#cite_note-AVII11-107">[106]</a></sup> This behaviour cost him the sympathies of many of his countrymen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA45_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA45-234">[233]</a></sup> However, Alexander also was a pragmatic ruler who understood the difficulties of ruling culturally disparate peoples, many of whom lived in kingdoms where the king was divine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-235"><a href="#cite_note-235">[234]</a></sup> Thus, rather than megalomania, his behaviour may simply have been a practical attempt at strengthening his rule and keeping his empire together.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[235]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_relationships">Personal relationships</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Personal_relationships_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Personal relationships of Alexander the Great">Personal relationships of Alexander the Great</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marriage_of_Alexander_and_Statira_as_Ares_and_Aphrodite,_fresco,_ca._69_d._C.,_copy_after_Aetion._Antiquarim,_Pompeii.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="587" data-file-width="468" decoding="async" height="276" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Marriage_of_Alexander_and_Statira_as_Ares_and_Aphrodite%2C_fresco%2C_ca._69_d._C.%2C_copy_after_Aetion._Antiquarim%2C_Pompeii.jpg/220px-Marriage_of_Alexander_and_Statira_as_Ares_and_Aphrodite%2C_fresco%2C_ca._69_d._C.%2C_copy_after_Aetion._Antiquarim%2C_Pompeii.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Marriage_of_Alexander_and_Statira_as_Ares_and_Aphrodite%2C_fresco%2C_ca._69_d._C.%2C_copy_after_Aetion._Antiquarim%2C_Pompeii.jpg/330px-Marriage_of_Alexander_and_Statira_as_Ares_and_Aphrodite%2C_fresco%2C_ca._69_d._C.%2C_copy_after_Aetion._Antiquarim%2C_Pompeii.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Marriage_of_Alexander_and_Statira_as_Ares_and_Aphrodite%2C_fresco%2C_ca._69_d._C.%2C_copy_after_Aetion._Antiquarim%2C_Pompeii.jpg/440px-Marriage_of_Alexander_and_Statira_as_Ares_and_Aphrodite%2C_fresco%2C_ca._69_d._C.%2C_copy_after_Aetion._Antiquarim%2C_Pompeii.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marriage_of_Alexander_and_Statira_as_Ares_and_Aphrodite,_fresco,_ca._69_d._C.,_copy_after_Aetion._Antiquarim,_Pompeii.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A mural in <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a>, depicting the marriage of Alexander to <a href="/wiki/Barsine" title="Barsine">Barsine</a> (<a href="/wiki/Stateira_(wife_of_Alexander_the_Great)" title="Stateira (wife of Alexander the Great)">Stateira</a>) in 324 BC; the couple are apparently dressed as <a href="/wiki/Ares" title="Ares">Ares</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aphrodite" title="Aphrodite">Aphrodite</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Alexander married three times: <a href="/wiki/Roxana" title="Roxana">Roxana</a>, daughter of the <a href="/wiki/Sogdia" title="Sogdia">Sogdian</a> nobleman <a href="/wiki/Oxyartes" title="Oxyartes">Oxyartes</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[236]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-238"><a href="#cite_note-238">[237]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[238]</a></sup> out of love;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1919[httpswwwperseustuftseduhoppertextdocPerseus3Atext3A19990102433Achapter3D47_LXVII,_1]_240-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1919[httpswwwperseustuftseduhoppertextdocPerseus3Atext3A19990102433Achapter3D47_LXVII,_1]-240">[239]</a></sup> and the Persian princesses <a href="/wiki/Stateira_(wife_of_Alexander_the_Great)" title="Stateira (wife of Alexander the Great)">Stateira</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parysatis_II" title="Parysatis II">Parysatis</a>, the former a daughter of <a href="/wiki/Darius_III" title="Darius III">Darius III</a> and the latter a daughter of <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_III" title="Artaxerxes III">Artaxerxes III</a>, for political reasons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carney_241-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carney-241">[240]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1936[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsPlutarchMoraliaFortuna_Alexandri2html_II,_6]_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1936[httpspenelopeuchicagoeduThayerERomanTextsPlutarchMoraliaFortuna_Alexandri2html_II,_6]-242">[241]</a></sup> He apparently had two sons, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_IV_of_Macedon" title="Alexander IV of Macedon">Alexander IV of Macedon</a> by Roxana and, possibly, <a href="/wiki/Heracles_of_Macedon" title="Heracles of Macedon">Heracles of Macedon</a> from his mistress <a href="/wiki/Barsine" title="Barsine">Barsine</a>. He lost another child when Roxana miscarried at Babylon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[242]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERenault2001100_244-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERenault2001100-244">[243]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alexander also had a close relationship with his friend, general, and bodyguard <a href="/wiki/Hephaestion" title="Hephaestion">Hephaestion</a>, the son of a Macedonian noble.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII14_145-2"><a href="#cite_note-AVII14-145">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200715–16_218-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200715–16-218">[217]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSXVII114_245-0"><a href="#cite_note-DSXVII114-245">[244]</a></sup> Hephaestion's death devastated Alexander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII14_145-3"><a href="#cite_note-AVII14-145">[144]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P72_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-P72-246">[245]</a></sup> This event may have contributed to Alexander's failing health and detached <a href="/wiki/Mental_health" title="Mental health">mental state</a> during his final months.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200723–24_155-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200723–24-155">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMD_164-2"><a href="#cite_note-AMD-164">[163]</a></sup>
</p><p>Alexander's sexuality has been the subject of speculation and controversy in modern times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOgden2009204_247-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOgden2009204-247">[246]</a></sup> The Roman era writer <a href="/wiki/Athenaeus" title="Athenaeus">Athenaeus</a> says, based on the scholar <a href="/wiki/Dicaearchus" title="Dicaearchus">Dicaearchus</a>, who was Alexander's contemporary, that the king "was quite excessively keen on boys", and that Alexander kissed the eunuch Bagoas in public.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[247]</a></sup> This episode is also told by Plutarch, probably based on the same source. None of Alexander's contemporaries, however, are known to have explicitly described Alexander's relationship with Hephaestion as sexual, though the pair was often compared to <a href="/wiki/Achilles_and_Patroclus" title="Achilles and Patroclus">Achilles and Patroclus</a>, whom classical Greek culture painted as a couple. Aelian writes of Alexander's visit to <a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">Troy</a> where "Alexander garlanded the tomb of Achilles, and Hephaestion that of <a href="/wiki/Patroclus" title="Patroclus">Patroclus</a>, the latter hinting that he was a beloved of Alexander, in just the same way as Patroclus was of Achilles."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AelXII7_249-0"><a href="#cite_note-AelXII7-249">[248]</a></sup> Some modern historians (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Robin_Lane_Fox" title="Robin Lane Fox">Robin Lane Fox</a>) believe not only that Alexander's youthful relationship with Hephaestion was sexual, but that their sexual contacts may have continued into adulthood, which went against the social norms of at least some Greek cities, such as Athens,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[249]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESacks199516_251-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESacks199516-251">[250]</a></sup> though some modern researchers have tentatively proposed that Macedonia (or at least the Macedonian court) may have been more tolerant of homosexuality between adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-252"><a href="#cite_note-252">[251]</a></sup>
</p><p>Green argues that there is little evidence in ancient sources that Alexander had much carnal interest in women; he did not produce an heir until the very end of his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200715–16_218-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200715–16-218">[217]</a></sup> However, Ogden calculates that Alexander, who impregnated his partners thrice in eight years, had a higher matrimonial record than his father at the same age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOgden2009208_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOgden2009208-253">[252]</a></sup> Two of these pregnancies — Stateira's and Barsine's — are of dubious legitimacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-254"><a href="#cite_note-254">[253]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Diodorus Siculus, Alexander accumulated a harem in the style of Persian kings, but he used it rather sparingly, "not wishing to offend the Macedonians",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DSXVII77_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-DSXVII77-255">[254]</a></sup> showing great self-control in "pleasures of the body".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AVII28_225-1"><a href="#cite_note-AVII28-225">[224]</a></sup> Nevertheless, Plutarch described how Alexander was infatuated by Roxana while complimenting him on not forcing himself on her.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-256"><a href="#cite_note-256">[255]</a></sup> Green suggested that, in the context of the period, Alexander formed quite strong friendships with women, including <a href="/wiki/Ada_of_Caria" title="Ada of Caria">Ada of Caria</a>, who adopted him, and even Darius's mother <a href="/wiki/Sisygambis" title="Sisygambis">Sisygambis</a>, who supposedly died from grief upon hearing of Alexander's death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200715–16_218-7"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200715–16-218">[217]</a></sup>
</p>

</p>
