<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mappa_di_Eratostene.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3096" data-file-width="5085" decoding="async" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Mappa_di_Eratostene.jpg/220px-Mappa_di_Eratostene.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Mappa_di_Eratostene.jpg/330px-Mappa_di_Eratostene.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Mappa_di_Eratostene.jpg/440px-Mappa_di_Eratostene.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mappa_di_Eratostene.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic" title="Hellenistic">Hellenistic</a> world view: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_world_map" title="Ancient world map">world map</a> of <a href="/wiki/Eratosthenes" title="Eratosthenes">Eratosthenes</a> (276–194 BC), using information from the campaigns of Alexander and his successors<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-257"><a href="#cite_note-257">[256]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Alexander's legacy extended beyond his military conquests, and his reign marked a turning point in European and Asian history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-258"><a href="#cite_note-258">[257]</a></sup> His campaigns greatly increased contacts and trade between <a href="/wiki/Eastern_world" title="Eastern world">East</a> and <a href="/wiki/Western_world" title="Western world">West</a>, and vast areas to the east were significantly exposed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_civilization" title="Greek civilization">Greek civilization</a> and influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186-18">[17]</a></sup> Some of the cities he founded became major cultural centers, many surviving into the 21st century. His chroniclers recorded valuable information about the areas through which he marched, while the Greeks themselves got a sense of belonging to a world beyond the Mediterranean.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hellenistic_kingdoms">Hellenistic kingdoms</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plan_of_Alexandria_c_30_BC_Otto_Puchstein_1890s_EN.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="547" data-file-width="682" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Plan_of_Alexandria_c_30_BC_Otto_Puchstein_1890s_EN.svg/220px-Plan_of_Alexandria_c_30_BC_Otto_Puchstein_1890s_EN.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Plan_of_Alexandria_c_30_BC_Otto_Puchstein_1890s_EN.svg/330px-Plan_of_Alexandria_c_30_BC_Otto_Puchstein_1890s_EN.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Plan_of_Alexandria_c_30_BC_Otto_Puchstein_1890s_EN.svg/440px-Plan_of_Alexandria_c_30_BC_Otto_Puchstein_1890s_EN.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plan_of_Alexandria_c_30_BC_Otto_Puchstein_1890s_EN.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Plan of Alexandria <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;"> 30 BC</span></div></div></div>
<p>Alexander's most immediate legacy was the introduction of Macedonian rule to huge new swathes of Asia. At the time of his death, Alexander's empire covered some 5,200,000 km<sup>2</sup> (2,000,000 sq mi),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uconn_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-uconn-259">[258]</a></sup> and was the largest state of its time. Many of these areas remained in Macedonian hands or under Greek influence for the next 200–300 years. The <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period#The_successors" title="Hellenistic period">successor states</a> that emerged were, at least initially, dominant forces, and these 300 years are often referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_period" title="Hellenistic period">Hellenistic period</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen2007xii–xix_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen2007xii–xix-260">[259]</a></sup>
</p><p>The eastern borders of Alexander's empire began to collapse even during his lifetime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200724–26_183-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200724–26-183">[182]</a></sup> However, the power vacuum he left in the northwest of the Indian subcontinent directly gave rise to one of the most powerful Indian dynasties in history, the <a href="/wiki/Maurya_Empire" title="Maurya Empire">Maurya Empire</a>. Taking advantage of this power vacuum, <a href="/wiki/Chandragupta_Maurya" title="Chandragupta Maurya">Chandragupta Maurya</a> (referred to in Greek sources as "Sandrokottos"), of relatively humble origin, took control of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjab_region" title="Punjab region">Punjab</a>, and with that power base proceeded to conquer the <a href="/wiki/Nanda_Empire" title="Nanda Empire">Nanda Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeay200182–85_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeay200182–85-261">[260]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Founding_of_cities">Founding of cities</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_founded_by_Alexander_the_Great" title="List of cities founded by Alexander the Great">List of cities founded by Alexander the Great</a></div>
<p>Over the course of his conquests, Alexander founded some <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Alexandria_(disambiguation)#West_and_Central_Asia" title="Alexandria (disambiguation)">twenty cities that bore his name</a>, most of them east of the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111-108">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livius_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-livius-262">[261]</a></sup> The first, and greatest, was <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> in Egypt, which would become one of the leading Mediterranean cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111_108-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111-108">[107]</a></sup> The cities' locations reflected trade routes as well as defensive positions. At first, the cities must have been inhospitable, little more than defensive garrisons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111_108-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111-108">[107]</a></sup> Following Alexander's death, many Greeks who had settled there tried to return to Greece.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111_108-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111-108">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livius_262-1"><a href="#cite_note-livius-262">[261]</a></sup> However, a century or so after Alexander's death, many of the Alexandrias were thriving, with elaborate public buildings and substantial populations that included both Greek and local peoples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111_108-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorkot1996111-108">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>The foundation of the "new" <a href="/wiki/Smyrna" title="Smyrna">Smyrna</a> was also associated with Alexander. According to the legend, after Alexander hunted on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mount_Pagus" title="Mount Pagus">Mount Pagus</a>, he slept under a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plane_tree" title="Plane tree">plane tree</a> at the sanctuary of <a href="/wiki/Nemesis" title="Nemesis">Nemesis</a>. While he was sleeping, the goddess appeared and told him to found a city there and move into it the Smyrnaeans from the "old" city. The Smyrnaeans sent ambassadors to the oracle at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clarus" title="Clarus">Clarus</a> to ask about this, and after the response from the oracle they decided to move to the "new" city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-paus75_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-paus75-263">[262]</a></sup>
</p><p>The city of <a href="/wiki/Pella,_Jordan" title="Pella, Jordan">Pella</a>, in modern <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, was founded by veterans of Alexander's army, and named it after the city of <a href="/wiki/Pella" title="Pella">Pella</a>, in Greece, which was the birthplace of Alexander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-264"><a href="#cite_note-264">[263]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Funding_of_temples">Funding of temples</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dedication_of_Alexander_the_Great_to_Athena_Polias_at_Priene.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3024" data-file-width="4032" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Dedication_of_Alexander_the_Great_to_Athena_Polias_at_Priene.jpg/220px-Dedication_of_Alexander_the_Great_to_Athena_Polias_at_Priene.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Dedication_of_Alexander_the_Great_to_Athena_Polias_at_Priene.jpg/330px-Dedication_of_Alexander_the_Great_to_Athena_Polias_at_Priene.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Dedication_of_Alexander_the_Great_to_Athena_Polias_at_Priene.jpg/440px-Dedication_of_Alexander_the_Great_to_Athena_Polias_at_Priene.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dedication_of_Alexander_the_Great_to_Athena_Polias_at_Priene.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dedication of Alexander the Great to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athena_Polias" title="Athena Polias">Athena Polias</a> at <a href="/wiki/Priene" title="Priene">Priene</a>, now housed in the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burn_265-0"><a href="#cite_note-Burn-265">[264]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In 334 BC, Alexander the Great donated funds for the completion of the new temple of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athena_Polias" title="Athena Polias">Athena Polias</a> in <a href="/wiki/Priene" title="Priene">Priene</a>, in modern-day western Turkey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritishMuseum_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-BritishMuseum-266">[265]</a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/Priene_Inscription" title="Priene Inscription">inscription from the temple</a>, now housed in the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>, declares: "King Alexander dedicated [this temple] to Athena Polias."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burn_265-1"><a href="#cite_note-Burn-265">[264]</a></sup> This inscription is one of the few independent archaeological discoveries confirming an episode from Alexander's life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burn_265-2"><a href="#cite_note-Burn-265">[264]</a></sup> The temple was designed by <a href="/wiki/Pythius_of_Priene" title="Pythius of Priene">Pytheos</a>, one of the architects of the <a href="/wiki/Mausoleum_at_Halicarnassus" title="Mausoleum at Halicarnassus">Mausoleum at Halicarnassus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Burn_265-3"><a href="#cite_note-Burn-265">[264]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BritishMuseum_266-1"><a href="#cite_note-BritishMuseum-266">[265]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-267"><a href="#cite_note-267">[266]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Libanius" title="Libanius">Libanius</a> wrote that Alexander founded the temple of Zeus Bottiaios (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">Βοττιαίου Δῖός</span>), in the place where later the city of <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a> was built.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-268"><a href="#cite_note-268">[267]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-269"><a href="#cite_note-269">[268]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Suda" title="Suda">Suda</a> wrote that Alexander built a big temple to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sarapis" title="Sarapis">Sarapis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[269]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hellenization">Hellenization</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hellenization" title="Hellenization">Hellenization</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander-Empire_323bc.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1288" data-file-width="2321" decoding="async" height="122" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Alexander-Empire_323bc.jpg/220px-Alexander-Empire_323bc.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Alexander-Empire_323bc.jpg/330px-Alexander-Empire_323bc.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Alexander-Empire_323bc.jpg/440px-Alexander-Empire_323bc.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander-Empire_323bc.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alexander's empire was the largest state of its time, covering approximately 5.2 million square km.</div></div></div>
<p><i>Hellenization</i> was coined by the German historian <a href="/wiki/Johann_Gustav_Droysen" title="Johann Gustav Droysen">Johann Gustav Droysen</a> to denote the spread of Greek language, culture, and population into the former Persian empire after Alexander's conquest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen2007xii–xix_260-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen2007xii–xix-260">[259]</a></sup> This process can be seen in such great Hellenistic cities as <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200756–59_271-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200756–59-271">[270]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Seleucia" title="Seleucia">Seleucia</a> (south of modern <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[271]</a></sup> Alexander sought to insert Greek elements into <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Iran" title="Culture of Iran">Persian culture</a> and to hybridize Greek and Persian culture, homogenizing the populations of Asia and Europe. Although his successors explicitly rejected such policies, Hellenization occurred throughout the region, accompanied by a distinct and opposite 'Orientalization' of the successor states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200721,_56–59_273-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200721,_56–59-273">[272]</a></sup>
</p><p>The core of the Hellenistic culture promulgated by the conquests was essentially <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athenian" title="Athenian">Athenian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-274"><a href="#cite_note-274">[273]</a></sup> The close association of men from across Greece in Alexander's army directly led to the emergence of the largely <a href="/wiki/Attic_Greek" title="Attic Greek">Attic</a>-based "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Koine" title="Koine">koine</a>", or "common" Greek dialect.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison197151_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison197151-275">[274]</a></sup> Koine spread throughout the Hellenistic world, becoming the <a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a> of Hellenistic lands and eventually the ancestor of <a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">modern Greek</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarrison197151_275-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarrison197151-275">[274]</a></sup> Furthermore, <a href="/wiki/Urban_planning" title="Urban planning">town planning</a>, education, local government, and art current in the Hellenistic period were all based on Classical Greek ideals, evolving into distinct new forms commonly grouped as Hellenistic. Also, the <a href="/wiki/New_Testament" title="New Testament">New Testament</a> was written in the <a href="/wiki/Koine_Greek" title="Koine Greek">Koine Greek</a> language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreen200756–59_271-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreen200756–59-271">[270]</a></sup> Aspects of Hellenistic culture were still evident in the traditions of the Byzantine Empire in the mid-15th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-276"><a href="#cite_note-276">[275]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Hellenization_in_South_and_Central_Asia">Hellenization in South and Central Asia</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Indo-Greek_Kingdom" title="Indo-Greek Kingdom">Indo-Greek Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indo-Greek_art" title="Indo-Greek art">Indo-Greek art</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhism" title="Greco-Buddhism">Greco-Buddhism</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gandhara_Buddha_(tnm).jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2894" data-file-width="1746" decoding="async" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg/170px-Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg/255px-Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg/340px-Gandhara_Buddha_%28tnm%29.jpeg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gandhara_Buddha_(tnm).jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Buddha</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhist_art" title="Greco-Buddhist art">Greco-Buddhist style</a>, 1st to 2nd century AD, <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandhara</a>, northern Pakistan. <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_National_Museum" title="Tokyo National Museum">Tokyo National Museum</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Some of the most pronounced effects of Hellenization can be seen in Afghanistan and India, in the region of the relatively late-rising <a href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian_Kingdom" title="Greco-Bactrian Kingdom">Greco-Bactrian Kingdom</a> (250–125 BC) (in modern <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>) and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Greeks" title="Indo-Greeks">Indo-Greek Kingdom</a> (180 BC – 10 AD) in modern Afghanistan and India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeay2001101–09_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeay2001101–09-277">[276]</a></sup> On the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> trade routes, Hellenistic culture hybridized with Iranian and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> cultures. The cosmopolitan art and mythology of <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandhara</a> (a region spanning the upper confluence of the Indus, Swat and Kabul rivers in modern Pakistan) of the ~3rd century BC to the ~5th century AD are most evident of the direct contact between Hellenistic civilization and South Asia, as are the <a href="/wiki/Edicts_of_Ashoka" title="Edicts of Ashoka">Edicts of Ashoka</a>, which directly mention the Greeks within Ashoka's dominion as converting to Buddhism and the reception of Buddhist emissaries by Ashoka's contemporaries in the Hellenistic world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[277]</a></sup> The resulting <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretism</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhism" title="Greco-Buddhism">Greco-Buddhism</a> influenced the development of Buddhism<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-279"><a href="#cite_note-279">[278]</a></sup> and created a culture of <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhist_art" title="Greco-Buddhist art">Greco-Buddhist art</a>. These Greco-Buddhist kingdoms sent some of the first Buddhist missionaries to <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> and Hellenistic Asia and Europe (<a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhist_monasticism" title="Greco-Buddhist monasticism">Greco-Buddhist monasticism</a>).
</p><p>Some of the first and most influential figurative portrayals of the <a href="/wiki/Gautama_Buddha" title="Gautama Buddha">Buddha</a> appeared at this time, perhaps modelled on Greek statues of <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a> in the Greco-Buddhist style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeay2001101–09_277-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeay2001101–09-277">[276]</a></sup> Several Buddhist traditions may have been influenced by the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_religion" title="Ancient Greek religion">ancient Greek religion</a>: the concept of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boddhisatvas" title="Boddhisatvas">Boddhisatvas</a> is reminiscent of Greek divine heroes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-280"><a href="#cite_note-280">[279]</a></sup> and some <a href="/wiki/Mahayana" title="Mahayana">Mahayana</a> <a href="/wiki/Offering_(Buddhism)" title="Offering (Buddhism)">ceremonial practices</a> (burning <a href="/wiki/Incense" title="Incense">incense</a>, gifts of flowers, and food placed on altars) are similar to those practised by the ancient Greeks; however, similar practices were also observed amongst the native Indic culture. One Greek king, <a href="/wiki/Menander_I" title="Menander I">Menander I</a>, probably became Buddhist, and was immortalized in <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_texts" title="Buddhist texts">Buddhist literature</a> as 'Milinda'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKeay2001101–09_277-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKeay2001101–09-277">[276]</a></sup> The process of Hellenization also spurred trade between the east and west.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pingree_(1978),_533,_554f._281-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pingree_(1978),_533,_554f.-281">[280]</a></sup> For example, Greek astronomical instruments dating to the 3rd century BC were found in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian" title="Greco-Bactrian">Greco-Bactrian</a> city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ai_Khanoum" title="Ai Khanoum">Ai Khanoum</a> in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-282"><a href="#cite_note-282">[281]</a></sup> while the Greek concept of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spherical_earth" title="Spherical earth">spherical earth</a> surrounded by the spheres of planets eventually supplanted the long-standing Indian cosmological belief of a disc consisting of four continents grouped around a central mountain (Mount Meru) like the petals of a flower.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pingree_(1978),_533,_554f._281-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pingree_(1978),_533,_554f.-281">[280]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-283"><a href="#cite_note-283">[282]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hayashi08Aryabhata_284-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hayashi08Aryabhata-284">[283]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Yavanajataka" title="Yavanajataka">Yavanajataka</a> (lit. Greek astronomical treatise) and <a href="/wiki/Paulisa_Siddhanta" title="Paulisa Siddhanta">Paulisa Siddhanta</a> texts depict the influence of Greek astronomical ideas on Indian astronomy.
</p><p>Following the conquests of Alexander the Great in the east, <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_influence_on_Indian_art" title="Hellenistic influence on Indian art">Hellenistic influence on Indian art</a> was far-ranging. In the area of <a href="/wiki/Architecture" title="Architecture">architecture</a>, a few examples of the <a href="/wiki/Ionic_order" title="Ionic order">Ionic order</a> can be found as far as <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Jandial" title="Jandial">Jandial temple</a> near <a href="/wiki/Taxila" title="Taxila">Taxila</a>. Several examples of <a href="/wiki/Capital_(architecture)" title="Capital (architecture)">capitals</a> displaying Ionic influences can be seen as far as <a href="/wiki/Patna" title="Patna">Patna</a>, especially with the <a href="/wiki/Pataliputra_capital" title="Pataliputra capital">Pataliputra capital</a>, dated to the 3rd century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-285"><a href="#cite_note-285">[284]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Corinthian_order" title="Corinthian order">Corinthian order</a> is also heavily represented in the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhist_art" title="Greco-Buddhist art">art of Gandhara</a>, especially through <a href="/wiki/Indo-Corinthian_capital" title="Indo-Corinthian capital">Indo-Corinthian capitals</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_on_Rome">Influence on Rome</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Roman_-_Medallion_with_Alexander_the_Great_-_Walters_591_-_Obverse.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1800" data-file-width="1711" decoding="async" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Roman_-_Medallion_with_Alexander_the_Great_-_Walters_591_-_Obverse.jpg/220px-Roman_-_Medallion_with_Alexander_the_Great_-_Walters_591_-_Obverse.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Roman_-_Medallion_with_Alexander_the_Great_-_Walters_591_-_Obverse.jpg/330px-Roman_-_Medallion_with_Alexander_the_Great_-_Walters_591_-_Obverse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/Roman_-_Medallion_with_Alexander_the_Great_-_Walters_591_-_Obverse.jpg/440px-Roman_-_Medallion_with_Alexander_the_Great_-_Walters_591_-_Obverse.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Roman_-_Medallion_with_Alexander_the_Great_-_Walters_591_-_Obverse.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>This medallion was produced in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_Rome" title="Imperial Rome">Imperial Rome</a>, demonstrating the influence of Alexander's memory. <a href="/wiki/Walters_Art_Museum" title="Walters Art Museum">Walters Art Museum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">Baltimore</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Alexander and his exploits were admired by many Romans, especially generals, who wanted to associate themselves with his achievements.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asirvatham_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-Asirvatham-286">[285]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Polybius" title="Polybius">Polybius</a> began his <i><a href="/wiki/The_Histories_(Polybius)" title="The Histories (Polybius)">Histories</a></i> by reminding Romans of Alexander's achievements, and thereafter Roman leaders saw him as a role model. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pompey_the_Great" title="Pompey the Great">Pompey the Great</a> adopted the epithet "Magnus" and even Alexander's anastole-type haircut, and searched the conquered lands of the east for Alexander's 260-year-old cloak, which he then wore as a sign of greatness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asirvatham_286-1"><a href="#cite_note-Asirvatham-286">[285]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> dedicated a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lysippus" title="Lysippus">Lysippean</a> <a href="/wiki/Equestrian_statue" title="Equestrian statue">equestrian</a> <a href="/wiki/Bronze" title="Bronze">bronze</a> statue but replaced Alexander's head with his own, while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Octavian" title="Octavian">Octavian</a> visited Alexander's tomb in Alexandria and temporarily changed his seal from a <a href="/wiki/Sphinx" title="Sphinx">sphinx</a> to Alexander's profile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asirvatham_286-2"><a href="#cite_note-Asirvatham-286">[285]</a></sup> The emperor <a href="/wiki/Trajan" title="Trajan">Trajan</a> also admired Alexander, as did <a href="/wiki/Nero" title="Nero">Nero</a> and <a href="/wiki/Caracalla" title="Caracalla">Caracalla</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asirvatham_286-3"><a href="#cite_note-Asirvatham-286">[285]</a></sup> The Macriani, a Roman family that in the person of <a href="/wiki/Macrinus" title="Macrinus">Macrinus</a> briefly ascended to the imperial throne, kept images of Alexander on their persons, either on jewellery, or embroidered into their clothes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolt20033_287-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolt20033-287">[286]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the other hand, some Roman writers, particularly Republican figures, used Alexander as a cautionary tale of how <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autocratic" title="Autocratic">autocratic</a> tendencies can be kept in check by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_republic" title="Roman republic">republican</a> values.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asirvatham2_288-0"><a href="#cite_note-Asirvatham2-288">[287]</a></sup> Alexander was used by these writers as an example of ruler values such as <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">amicita</i></span> (friendship) and <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">clementia</i></span> (clemency), but also <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">iracundia</i></span> (anger) and <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">cupiditas gloriae</i></span> (over-desire for glory).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Asirvatham2_288-1"><a href="#cite_note-Asirvatham2-288">[287]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Julian_(emperor)" title="Julian (emperor)">Emperor Julian</a> in his satire called "The Caesars", describes a contest between the previous Roman emperors, with Alexander the Great called in as an extra contestant, in the presence of the assembled gods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-289"><a href="#cite_note-289">[288]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Itinerarium_Alexandri" title="Itinerarium Alexandri">Itinerarium Alexandri</a> is a 4th-century Latin <a href="/wiki/Itinerarium" title="Itinerarium">Itinerarium</a> which describes Alexander the Great's campaigns. <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> went to serve his quaestorship in Hispania after his wife's funeral, in the spring or early summer of 69 BC. While there, he encountered a statue of Alexander the Great, and realised with dissatisfaction that he was now at an age when Alexander had the world at his feet, while he had achieved comparatively little.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[289]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-291"><a href="#cite_note-291">[290]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pompey" title="Pompey">Pompey</a> posed as the "new Alexander" since he was his boyhood hero.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-292"><a href="#cite_note-292">[291]</a></sup>
</p><p>After Caracalla concluded his campaign against the Alamanni, it became evident that he was inordinately preoccupied with Alexander the Great.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_293-0"><a href="#cite_note-:02-293">[292]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:92_294-0"><a href="#cite_note-:92-294">[293]</a></sup> He began openly mimicking Alexander in his personal style. In planning his invasion of the Parthian Empire, Caracalla decided to arrange 16,000 of his men in Macedonian-style <a href="/wiki/Phalanx" title="Phalanx">phalanxes</a>, despite the Roman army having made the phalanx an obsolete tactical formation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_293-1"><a href="#cite_note-:02-293">[292]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:92_294-1"><a href="#cite_note-:92-294">[293]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:292_295-0"><a href="#cite_note-:292-295">[294]</a></sup> The historian Christopher Matthew mentions that the term <i>Phalangarii</i> has two possible meanings, both with military connotations. The first refers merely to the Roman battle line and does not specifically mean that the men were armed with <a href="/wiki/Pike_(weapon)" title="Pike (weapon)">pikes</a>, and the second bears similarity to the 'Marian Mules' of the late <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> who carried their equipment suspended from a long pole, which were in use until at least the 2nd century AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:292_295-1"><a href="#cite_note-:292-295">[294]</a></sup> As a consequence, the <i>Phalangarii</i> of <a href="/wiki/Legio_II_Parthica" title="Legio II Parthica">Legio II Parthica</a> may not have been pikemen, but rather standard battle line troops or possibly <i><a href="/wiki/Triarii" title="Triarii">Triarii</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:292_295-2"><a href="#cite_note-:292-295">[294]</a></sup>
</p><p>Caracalla's mania for Alexander went so far that Caracalla visited Alexandria while preparing for his Persian invasion and persecuted philosophers of the <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelian</a> school based on a legend that <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> had poisoned Alexander. This was a sign of Caracalla's increasingly erratic behaviour. But this mania for Alexander, strange as it was, was overshadowed by subsequent events in Alexandria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:92_294-2"><a href="#cite_note-:92-294">[293]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 39, Caligula performed a spectacular stunt by ordering a temporary <a href="/wiki/Pontoon_bridge" title="Pontoon bridge">floating bridge</a> to be built using ships as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pontoon_(boat)" title="Pontoon (boat)">pontoons</a>, stretching for over two miles from the resort of <a href="/wiki/Baiae" title="Baiae">Baiae</a> to the neighbouring port of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Puteoli" title="Puteoli">Puteoli</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-296"><a href="#cite_note-296">[295]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seutonius-calig-19_297-0"><a href="#cite_note-seutonius-calig-19-297">[296]</a></sup> It was said that the bridge was to rival the Persian king <a href="/wiki/Xerxes%27_Pontoon_Bridges" title="Xerxes' Pontoon Bridges">Xerxes' pontoon bridge</a> crossing of the Hellespont.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seutonius-calig-19_297-1"><a href="#cite_note-seutonius-calig-19-297">[296]</a></sup> Caligula, who could not swim,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-298"><a href="#cite_note-298">[297]</a></sup> then proceeded to ride his favourite horse <a href="/wiki/Incitatus" title="Incitatus">Incitatus</a> across, wearing the breastplate of <a href="#After_death">Alexander the Great</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seutonius-calig-19_297-2"><a href="#cite_note-seutonius-calig-19-297">[296]</a></sup> This act was in defiance of a prediction by Tiberius's soothsayer <a href="/wiki/Thrasyllus_of_Mendes" title="Thrasyllus of Mendes">Thrasyllus of Mendes</a> that Caligula had "no more chance of becoming emperor than of riding a horse across the Bay of Baiae".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seutonius-calig-19_297-3"><a href="#cite_note-seutonius-calig-19-297">[296]</a></sup>
</p><p>The diffusion of Greek culture and language cemented by Alexander's conquests in West Asia and North Africa served as a "precondition" for the <a href="/wiki/Mithridatic_Wars" title="Mithridatic Wars">later Roman expansion</a> into these territories and <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Greeks" title="Byzantine Greeks">entire basis</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>, according to <a href="/wiki/Robert_Malcolm_Errington" title="Robert Malcolm Errington">Errington</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-299"><a href="#cite_note-299">[298]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unsuccessful_plan_to_cut_a_canal_through_the_Anatolian_isthmus">Unsuccessful plan to cut a canal through the Anatolian isthmus</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pausanias_(geographer)" title="Pausanias (geographer)">Pausanias</a> writes that Alexander wanted to dig through the Mimas mountain (in today's <a href="/wiki/Karaburun" title="Karaburun">Karaburun</a> area), but didn't succeed. He says this was Alexander's only unsuccessful project.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-300"><a href="#cite_note-300">[299]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a> adds that the planned distance was 12 kilometres (7.5 mi), and the purpose was to cut a canal through the isthmus to connect the Caystrian and Hermaean bays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-301"><a href="#cite_note-301">[300]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-302"><a href="#cite_note-302">[301]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Naming_of_the_Icarus_island_in_the_Persian_Gulf">Naming of the Icarus island in the Persian Gulf</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Arrian" title="Arrian">Arrian</a> wrote that <a href="/wiki/Aristobulus_of_Cassandreia" title="Aristobulus of Cassandreia">Aristobulus</a> said that Alexander named <a href="/wiki/Ikaros_(Failaka_Island)" title="Ikaros (Failaka Island)">Icarus island</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Failaka_Island" title="Failaka Island">Failaka Island</a>) in the <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">Persian Gulf</a> after <a href="/wiki/Icaria" title="Icaria">Icarus island</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Sea" title="Aegean Sea">Aegean</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-303"><a href="#cite_note-303">[302]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-304"><a href="#cite_note-304">[303]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Letters">Letters</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Letters_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Letters of Alexander the Great">Letters of Alexander the Great</a></div>
<p>Alexander wrote and received numerous letters, but no <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autograph_manuscript" title="Autograph manuscript">originals</a> survive.  A few official letters addressed to the Greek cities survive in copies inscribed in stone and the content of others is sometimes reported in historical sources. These only occasionally quote the letters and it is an open question how reliable such quotations are. Several fictitious letters, some perhaps based on actual letters, made their way into the <i>Romance</i> tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LICP_305-0"><a href="#cite_note-LICP-305">[304]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_legend">In legend</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_romance._Armenian_illuminated_manuscript_of_XIV_century_(Venice,_San_Lazzaro,_424)_(1).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1181" data-file-width="857" decoding="async" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Alexander_romance._Armenian_illuminated_manuscript_of_XIV_century_%28Venice%2C_San_Lazzaro%2C_424%29_%281%29.jpg/170px-Alexander_romance._Armenian_illuminated_manuscript_of_XIV_century_%28Venice%2C_San_Lazzaro%2C_424%29_%281%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Alexander_romance._Armenian_illuminated_manuscript_of_XIV_century_%28Venice%2C_San_Lazzaro%2C_424%29_%281%29.jpg/255px-Alexander_romance._Armenian_illuminated_manuscript_of_XIV_century_%28Venice%2C_San_Lazzaro%2C_424%29_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Alexander_romance._Armenian_illuminated_manuscript_of_XIV_century_%28Venice%2C_San_Lazzaro%2C_424%29_%281%29.jpg/340px-Alexander_romance._Armenian_illuminated_manuscript_of_XIV_century_%28Venice%2C_San_Lazzaro%2C_424%29_%281%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_romance._Armenian_illuminated_manuscript_of_XIV_century_(Venice,_San_Lazzaro,_424)_(1).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alexander in a 14th-century Armenian manuscript</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great_in_legend" title="Alexander the Great in legend">Alexander the Great in legend</a></div>
<p>Many of the legends about Alexander derive from his own lifetime, probably encouraged by Alexander himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010187_306-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010187-306">[305]</a></sup> His court historian Callisthenes portrayed the sea in <a href="/wiki/Cilicia" title="Cilicia">Cilicia</a> as drawing back from him in proskynesis. Writing shortly after Alexander's death, <a href="/wiki/Onesicritus" title="Onesicritus">Onesicritus</a> invented a tryst between Alexander and <a href="/wiki/Thalestris" title="Thalestris">Thalestris</a>, queen of the mythical <a href="/wiki/Amazons" title="Amazons">Amazons</a>. He reportedly read this passage to his patron King <a href="/wiki/Lysimachus" title="Lysimachus">Lysimachus</a>, who had been one of Alexander's generals and who quipped, "I wonder where I was at the time."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PA46_307-0"><a href="#cite_note-PA46-307">[306]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the first centuries after Alexander's death, probably in Alexandria, a quantity of the legendary material coalesced into a text known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Alexander_Romance" title="Alexander Romance">Alexander Romance</a></i>, later falsely ascribed to Callisthenes and therefore known as <i>Pseudo-Callisthenes</i>. This text underwent numerous expansions and revisions throughout Antiquity and the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-308"><a href="#cite_note-308">[307]</a></sup> containing many dubious stories,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010187_306-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010187-306">[305]</a></sup> and was translated into numerous languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010117_309-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010117-309">[308]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_ancient_and_modern_culture">In ancient and modern culture</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Cultural_depictions_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Cultural depictions of Alexander the Great">Cultural depictions of Alexander the Great</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great_in_the_Quran" title="Alexander the Great in the Quran">Alexander the Great in the Quran</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Cataphract_(cropped).JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="884" data-file-width="1225" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Cataphract_%28cropped%29.JPG/220px-Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Cataphract_%28cropped%29.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Cataphract_%28cropped%29.JPG/330px-Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Cataphract_%28cropped%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Cataphract_%28cropped%29.JPG/440px-Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Cataphract_%28cropped%29.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Byzantine_Greek_Alexander_Manuscript_Cataphract_(cropped).JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alexander in a 14th-century Byzantine manuscript</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_the_Great_conquering_the_air.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="723" data-file-width="1036" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Alexander_the_Great_conquering_the_air.jpg/220px-Alexander_the_Great_conquering_the_air.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Alexander_the_Great_conquering_the_air.jpg/330px-Alexander_the_Great_conquering_the_air.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Alexander_the_Great_conquering_the_air.jpg/440px-Alexander_the_Great_conquering_the_air.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alexander_the_Great_conquering_the_air.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alexander conquering the air. <a href="/wiki/Jean_Wauquelin" title="Jean Wauquelin">Jean Wauquelin</a>, <i>Les faits et conquêtes d'Alexandre le Grand</i>, 1448–1449</div></div></div>
<p>Alexander the Great's accomplishments and legacy have been depicted in many cultures. Alexander has figured in both high and popular culture beginning in his own era to the present day. The <i>Alexander Romance</i>, in particular, has had a significant impact on portrayals of Alexander in later cultures, from Persian to medieval European to modern Greek.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010117_309-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010117-309">[308]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Khalili_Collection_Hajj_and_Arts_of_Pilgrimage_mss-0771_CROP.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="937" data-file-width="836" decoding="async" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Khalili_Collection_Hajj_and_Arts_of_Pilgrimage_mss-0771_CROP.jpg/170px-Khalili_Collection_Hajj_and_Arts_of_Pilgrimage_mss-0771_CROP.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Khalili_Collection_Hajj_and_Arts_of_Pilgrimage_mss-0771_CROP.jpg/255px-Khalili_Collection_Hajj_and_Arts_of_Pilgrimage_mss-0771_CROP.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Khalili_Collection_Hajj_and_Arts_of_Pilgrimage_mss-0771_CROP.jpg/340px-Khalili_Collection_Hajj_and_Arts_of_Pilgrimage_mss-0771_CROP.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Khalili_Collection_Hajj_and_Arts_of_Pilgrimage_mss-0771_CROP.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Folio from the <i><a href="/wiki/Shahnameh" title="Shahnameh">Shahnameh</a></i> showing Alexander praying at the <a href="/wiki/Kaaba" title="Kaaba">Kaaba</a>, mid-16th century</div></div></div><p>
Alexander features prominently in modern Greek folklore, more so than any other ancient figure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fermor_310-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fermor-310">[309]</a></sup> The colloquial form of his name in modern Greek ("O Megalexandros") is a household name, and he is the only ancient hero to appear in the <a href="/wiki/Karagiozis" title="Karagiozis">Karagiozis</a> shadow play.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fermor_310-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fermor-310">[309]</a></sup> One well-known fable among Greek seamen involves a solitary <a href="/wiki/Mermaid" title="Mermaid">mermaid</a> who would grasp a ship's prow during a storm and ask the captain "Is King Alexander alive?" The correct answer is "He is alive and well and rules the world!" causing the mermaid to vanish and the sea to calm. Any other answer would cause the mermaid to turn into a raging <a href="/wiki/Gorgon" title="Gorgon">Gorgon</a> who would drag the ship to the bottom of the sea, all hands aboard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fermor_310-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fermor-310">[309]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Submerge2.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1438" data-file-width="1950" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Submerge2.JPG/220px-Submerge2.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Submerge2.JPG/330px-Submerge2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Submerge2.JPG/440px-Submerge2.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Submerge2.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Detail of a 16th-century <a href="/wiki/Islamic_art" title="Islamic art">Islamic painting</a> depicting Alexander being lowered in a glass <a href="/wiki/Submersible" title="Submersible">submersible</a></div></div></div>
<p>In pre-Islamic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_Persian_language" title="Middle Persian language">Middle Persian</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zoroastrian" title="Zoroastrian">Zoroastrian</a>) literature, Alexander is referred to by the epithet <i>gujastak</i>, meaning "accursed", and is accused of destroying temples and burning the sacred texts of Zoroastrianism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-311"><a href="#cite_note-311">[310]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni Islamic</a> Persia, under the influence of the <i><a href="/wiki/Alexander_Romance" title="Alexander Romance">Alexander Romance</a></i> (in <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="fa">اسکندرنامه</span> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iskandarnamah" title="Iskandarnamah">Iskandarnamah</a></i>), a more positive portrayal of Alexander emerges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120_312-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120-312">[311]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Firdausi" title="Firdausi">Firdausi</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Shahnameh" title="Shahnameh">Shahnameh</a></i> ("The Book of Kings") includes Alexander in a line of legitimate Persian <a href="/wiki/Shah" title="Shah">shahs</a>, a mythical figure who explored the far reaches of the world in search of the <a href="/wiki/Fountain_of_Youth" title="Fountain of Youth">Fountain of Youth</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-313"><a href="#cite_note-313">[312]</a></sup> In the <i>Shahnameh</i>, Alexander's first journey is to <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a> to pray at the <a href="/wiki/Kaaba" title="Kaaba">Kaaba</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-314"><a href="#cite_note-314">[313]</a></sup> Alexander was depicted as performing a <a href="/wiki/Hajj" title="Hajj">Hajj</a> (pilgrimage to Mecca) many times in subsequent Islamic art and literature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-315"><a href="#cite_note-315">[314]</a></sup> Later Persian writers associate him with philosophy, portraying him at a symposium with figures such as <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> and Aristotle, in search of immortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120_312-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120-312">[311]</a></sup>
</p><p>The figure of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhul-Qarnayn" title="Dhul-Qarnayn">Dhul-Qarnayn</a> (literally "the Two-Horned One") mentioned in the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> is believed by scholars to be based on later legends of Alexander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120_312-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120-312">[311]</a></sup> In this tradition, he was a heroic figure who built a wall to defend against the nations of <a href="/wiki/Gog_and_Magog" title="Gog and Magog">Gog and Magog</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010122_316-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010122-316">[315]</a></sup> He then travelled the known world in search of the Water of Life and Immortality, eventually becoming a prophet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010122_316-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010122-316">[315]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac</a> version of the <i>Alexander Romance</i> portrays him as an ideal Christian world conqueror who prayed to "the one true God".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120_312-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120-312">[311]</a></sup> In Egypt, Alexander was portrayed as the son of <a href="/wiki/Nectanebo_II" title="Nectanebo II">Nectanebo II</a>, the last <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">pharaoh</a> before the Persian conquest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010122_316-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010122-316">[315]</a></sup> His defeat of Darius was depicted as Egypt's salvation, "proving" Egypt was still ruled by an Egyptian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120_312-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010120-312">[311]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Josephus" title="Josephus">Josephus</a>, Alexander was shown the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Daniel" title="Book of Daniel">Book of Daniel</a> when he entered Jerusalem, which described a mighty Greek king who would conquer the Persian Empire. This is cited as a reason for sparing Jerusalem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-317"><a href="#cite_note-317">[316]</a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a>, the name "<a href="/wiki/Iskandar" title="Iskandar">Sikandar</a>", derived from the Persian name for Alexander, denotes a rising young talent, and the <a href="/wiki/Delhi_Sultanate" title="Delhi Sultanate">Delhi Sultanate</a> ruler <a href="/wiki/Alauddin_Khalji" title="Alauddin Khalji">Aladdin Khalji</a> stylized himself as "Sikandar-i-Sani" (the Second Alexander the Great).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-318"><a href="#cite_note-318">[317]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Medieval_India" title="Medieval India">medieval India</a>, Turkic and Afghan sovereigns from the Iranian-cultured region of Central Asia brought positive cultural connotations of Alexander to the Indian subcontinent, resulting in the efflorescence of <i>Sikandernameh</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexander_romance" title="Alexander romance">Alexander Romances</a>) written by Indo-Persian poets such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amir_Khusrow" title="Amir Khusrow">Amir Khusrow</a> and the prominence of Alexander the Great as a popular subject in Mughal-era Persian miniatures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-319"><a href="#cite_note-319">[318]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">medieval Europe</a>, Alexander the Great was revered as a member of the <a href="/wiki/Nine_Worthies" title="Nine Worthies">Nine Worthies</a>, a group of heroes whose lives were believed to encapsulate all the ideal qualities of <a href="/wiki/Chivalry" title="Chivalry">chivalry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-320"><a href="#cite_note-320">[319]</a></sup> During the first <a href="/wiki/Italian_campaigns_of_the_French_Revolutionary_Wars" title="Italian campaigns of the French Revolutionary Wars">Italian campaign of the French Revolutionary Wars</a>, in a question from Bourrienne, asking whether he gave his preference to Alexander or Caesar, <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon</a> said that he places Alexander The Great in the first rank, the main reason being his campaign on Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-321"><a href="#cite_note-321">[320]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <i><a href="/wiki/Greek_Anthology" title="Greek Anthology">Greek Anthology</a></i>, there are poems referring to Alexander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-322"><a href="#cite_note-322">[321]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-323"><a href="#cite_note-323">[322]</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout time, art objects related to Alexander were being created. In addition to speech works, sculptures and paintings, in modern times Alexander is still the subject of musical and cinematic works. The song 'Alexander the Great' by the British heavy metal band <a href="/wiki/Iron_Maiden" title="Iron Maiden">Iron Maiden</a> is indicative. Some films that have been shot with the theme of Alexander are:
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Sikandar_(1941_film)" title="Sikandar (1941 film)">Sikandar</a></i> (1941), an Indian production directed by <a href="/wiki/Sohrab_Modi" title="Sohrab Modi">Sohrab Modi</a> about the conquest of India by Alexander<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-324"><a href="#cite_note-324">[323]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great_(1956_film)" title="Alexander the Great (1956 film)">Alexander the Great</a></i> (1956), produced by MGM and starring <a href="/wiki/Richard_Burton" title="Richard Burton">Richard Burton</a></li>
<li><i>Sikandar-e-Azam</i> (1965), an Indian production directed by Kedar Kapoor</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Alexander_(2004_film)" title="Alexander (2004 film)">Alexander</a></i> (2004), directed by <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Stone" title="Oliver Stone">Oliver Stone</a>, starring <a href="/wiki/Colin_Farrell" title="Colin Farrell">Colin Farrell</a></li></ul>
<p>There are also many references to other movies and TV series.
</p><p>Newer novels about Alexander are:
</p><p>The trilogy "Alexander the Great" by <a href="/wiki/Valerio_Massimo_Manfredi" title="Valerio Massimo Manfredi">Valerio Massimo Manfredi</a> consisting of "The son of the dream", "The sand of Amon", and "The ends of the world".
The trilogy of <a href="/wiki/Mary_Renault" title="Mary Renault">Mary Renault</a> consisting of "<a href="/wiki/Fire_from_Heaven" title="Fire from Heaven">Fire from Heaven</a>", "<a href="/wiki/The_Persian_Boy" title="The Persian Boy">The Persian Boy</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Funeral_Games_(novel)" title="Funeral Games (novel)">Funeral Games</a>".
</p>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Virtues_of_War" title="The Virtues of War">The Virtues of War</a></i>, about Alexander the Great (2004), <style <p>Irish playwright <a href="/wiki/Aubrey_Thomas_de_Vere" title="Aubrey Thomas de Vere">Aubrey Thomas de Vere</a> wrote <i><a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great,_a_Dramatic_Poem" title="Alexander the Great, a Dramatic Poem">Alexander the Great, a Dramatic Poem</a></i>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Historiography_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Historiography of Alexander the Great">Historiography of Alexander the Great</a></div>
<p>Apart from a few inscriptions and fragments, texts written by people who actually knew Alexander or who gathered information from men who served with Alexander were all lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186_18-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186-18">[17]</a></sup> Contemporaries who wrote accounts of his life included Alexander's campaign historian Callisthenes; Alexander's generals Ptolemy and <a href="/wiki/Nearchus" title="Nearchus">Nearchus</a>; <a href="/wiki/Aristobulus_of_Cassandreia" title="Aristobulus of Cassandreia">Aristobulus</a>, a junior officer on the campaigns; and Onesicritus, Alexander's chief helmsman. Their works are lost, but later works based on these <a href="/wiki/Primary_source" title="Primary source">original sources</a> have survived. The earliest of these is <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus Siculus</a> (1st century BC), followed by Quintus Curtius Rufus (mid-to-late 1st century AD), Arrian (1st to 2nd century AD), the biographer Plutarch (1st to 2nd century AD), and finally <a href="/wiki/Justin_(historian)" title="Justin (historian)">Justin</a>, whose work dated as late as the 4th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186_18-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186-18">[17]</a></sup> Of these, Arrian is generally considered the most reliable, given that he used Ptolemy and Aristobulus as his sources, closely followed by Diodorus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186_18-6"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERoismanWorthington2010186-18">[17]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h3>
<div style=""><ol class="references" style="list-style-type:upper-alpha;">
<li id="cnote_a"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#ref_a_1">^</a></b></span> <div class="reference-text" style="display:inline;">Macedon was an Ancient Greek polity. The Macedonians were a Greek tribe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Macedonians_325-0"><a href="#cite_note-Macedonians-325">[324]</a></sup> </div></li>
<li id="cnote_b"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#ref_b_1">^</a></b></span> <div class="reference-text" style="display:inline;"> By the time of his death, he had conquered the entire <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Persian Empire</a>, adding it to Macedon's European territories; according to some modern writers, this was most of the world then known to the ancient Greeks (the '<a href="/wiki/Ecumene#Ancient_world" title="Ecumene">Ecumene</a>').<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDanforth199738,_49,_167_326-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDanforth199738,_49,_167-326">[325]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStoneman20042_327-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoneman20042-327">[326]</a></sup> An approximate view of the world known to Alexander can be seen in <a href="/wiki/Hecataeus_of_Miletus" title="Hecataeus of Miletus">Hecataeus of Miletus</a>'s map; see <a href="/wiki/File:Hecataeus_world_map-en.svg" title="File:Hecataeus world map-en.svg">Hecataeus world map</a>. </div></li>
<li id="cnote_c"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#ref_c_1">^</a></b></span> <div class="reference-text" style="display:inline;"> For instance, <a href="/wiki/Hannibal" title="Hannibal">Hannibal</a> supposedly ranked Alexander as the greatest general;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGoldsworthy2003327–28_328-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoldsworthy2003327–28-328">[327]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a> wept on seeing a statue of Alexander, since he had achieved so little by the same age;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Plutarch,_Caesar,_11_329-0"><a href="#cite_note-Plutarch,_Caesar,_11-329">[328]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pompey" title="Pompey">Pompey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alauddin_Khalji" title="Alauddin Khalji">Alauddin Khalji</a> consciously posed as the 'new Alexander';<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHolland2003176–83_330-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHolland2003176–83-330">[329]</a></sup> the young <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_Bonaparte" title="Napoleon Bonaparte">Napoleon Bonaparte</a> also encouraged comparisons with Alexander. Napoleon also placed Alexander in the first rank.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarnett199745_331-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarnett199745-331">[330]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Caracalla" title="Caracalla">Caracalla</a> believed himself to be the actual reincarnation of Alexander.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-332"><a href="#cite_note-332">[331]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-333"><a href="#cite_note-333">[332]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-334"><a href="#cite_note-334">[333]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Caligula" title="Caligula">Caligula</a> wore the breastplate of Alexander in order to show his power.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-335"><a href="#cite_note-335">[334]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-336"><a href="#cite_note-336">[335]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Fidel Castro</a>'s hero was Alexander the Great, whose Spanish equivalent <i>Alejandro</i> he adopted as his <i>nom de guerre</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-337"><a href="#cite_note-337">[336]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mehmed_the_Conqueror" title="Mehmed the Conqueror">Mehmed the Conqueror</a>'s heroes were Alexander and <a href="/wiki/Achilles" title="Achilles">Achilles</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-338"><a href="#cite_note-338">[337]</a></sup></div></li>
<li id="cnote_d"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#ref_d_1">^</a></b></span> <div class="reference-text" style="display:inline;"> The name <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Ἀλέξανδρος</span></span> derives from the Greek verb <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ἀλέξω</span></span> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">aléxō</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Literal_translation" title="Literal translation">lit.</a><span style="white-space: nowrap;"> </span>'ward off, avert, defend')<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1919[httpswwwperseustuftseduhoppertextdocPerseus3Atext3A19990400573Aentry3Da29le2Fcw_IV,_57]_339-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1919[httpswwwperseustuftseduhoppertextdocPerseus3Atext3A19990400573Aentry3Da29le2Fcw_IV,_57]-339">[338]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiddellScott1940_340-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiddellScott1940-340">[339]</a></sup> and <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ἀνδρ-</span></span> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">andr-</i></span>), the stem of <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ἀνήρ</span></span> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">anḗr</i></span>, <abbr title="literal translation">lit.</abbr><span style="white-space: nowrap;"> </span>'man'),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1919[httpswwwperseustuftseduhoppertextdocPerseus3Atext3A19990400573Aentry3Da29nh2Fr_IV,_57]_341-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPlutarch1919[httpswwwperseustuftseduhoppertextdocPerseus3Atext3A19990400573Aentry3Da29nh2Fr_IV,_57]-341">[340]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELiddellScott1940_340-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELiddellScott1940-340">[339]</a></sup> and means "protector of men".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-342"><a href="#cite_note-342">[341]</a></sup> </div></li>
<li id="cnote_e"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#ref_e_1">^</a></b></span> <div class="reference-text" style="display:inline;"> There have been, since the time, many suspicions that Pausanias was actually hired to murder Philip. Suspicion has fallen upon Alexander, Olympias and even the newly crowned Persian Emperor, Darius III. All three of these people had motive to have Philip murdered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELane_Fox198072–73_343-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELane_Fox198072–73-343">[342]</a></sup> </div></li>
<li id="cnote_f"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#ref_f_1">^</a></b></span> <div class="reference-text" style="display:inline;"> However, <a href="/wiki/Arrian" title="Arrian">Arrian</a>, who used <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy_I_Soter" title="Ptolemy I Soter">Ptolemy</a> as a source, said that Alexander crossed with more than 5,000 horse and 30,000 foot; <a href="/wiki/Diodorus_Siculus" title="Diodorus Siculus">Diodorus</a> quoted the same totals, but listed 5,100 horse and 32,000 foot. Diodorus also referred to an advance force already present in Asia, which <a href="/wiki/Polyaenus" title="Polyaenus">Polyaenus</a>, in his Stratagems of War (5.44.4), said numbered 10,000 men.</div></li>
</ol></div>

<li id="cite_note-252"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-252">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFThomas_Hubbard2014">Thomas Hubbard (2014). Blackwell Publishing Ltd. p. 143. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-1-4051-9572-0" title="Special:BookSources/978-1-4051-9572-0"><bdi>978-1-4051-9572-0</bdi></a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Chapter+8%3A+Peer+Homosexuality&amp;rft.btitle=A+Companion+to+Greek+and+Roman+Sexualities&amp;rft.pages=143&amp;rft.pub=Blackwell+Publishing+Ltd&amp;rft.date=2014&amp;rft.isbn=978-1-4051-9572-0&amp;rft.au=Thomas+Hubbard&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAlexander+the+Great"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEOgden2009208-253"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOgden2009208_253-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFOgden2009">Ogden 2009</a>, p. 208... three attested pregnancies in eight years produces an attested impregnation rate of one every 2.7 years, which is actually superior to that of his father.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-254"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-254">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFMary_Renault1979"><a href="/wiki/Mary_Renault" title="Mary Renault">Mary Renault</a> (1979). <i><a href="/wiki/The_Nature_of_Alexander" title="The Nature of Alexander">The Nature of Alexander</a></i>. Pantheon. p. 110. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-394-73825-3" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-394-73825-3"><bdi>978-0-394-73825-3</bdi></a>. <q>No record at all exists of such a woman [ie, Barsine] accompanying his march; nor of any claim by her, or her powerful kin, that she had borne him offspring. Yet twelve years after his death a boy was produced, seventeen years old, born therefore five years after Damascus, her alleged son "brought up in Pergamon"; a claimant and shortlived pawn in the succession wars, chosen probably for a physical resemblance to Alexander. That he actually did marry another Barsine must have helped both to launch and preserve the story; but no source reports any notice whatever taken by him of a child who, Roxane's being posthumous, would have been during his lifetime his only son, by a near-royal mother. In a man who named cities after his horse and dog, this strains credulity.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+Nature+of+Alexander&amp;rft.pages=110&amp;rft.pub=Pantheon&amp;rft.date=1979&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-394-73825-3&amp;rft.au=Mary+Renault&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAlexander+the+Great"></span></span>
</li>
